Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
The Third Question: Pain from the Disagreeable
Al-Jurjani raises the third question about pain and discusses the proper ordering of arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third question raised against the philosophers: if God experiences pleasure from the agreeable, He would also experience pain from the disagreeable, which is impossible.

Core Idea
Pleasure and pain are opposites; if God can feel one, He must feel the other.

The author continues the refutation by raising a powerful third question. Even if we grant that God is receptive to pleasure and that His awareness is analogous to ours, a new problem emerges: if God feels pleasure from awareness of what is agreeable, He would necessarily feel pain from awareness of what is disagreeable—such as false doctrines like the Trinity. But God cannot experience pain, so the whole framework collapses.

Guided Path
1
Understand the third question
The author presents a conditional: if God feels pleasure from the agreeable, He must feel pain from the disagreeable. This is a logical consequence of the definition.
The example of the Trinity is given as something that would cause pain to God if He were aware of it.
2
Note the ordering of questions
The author comments that this third question should have been presented before the second, because denying God's receptivity should come after establishing that the cause is shared. This clarifies the logical flow.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The philosophers' position leads to an absurdity (luzūm al-muḥāl), so the original claim that God experiences pleasure is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد السابع : أنه تعالى لا يتصف بشيء وأثبتها الفلاسفة قالوا اللذة إدراك الملائم فمن أدرك كمالاً في ذاته التذ به وذلك ضوري) يشهد به الوجدان ( ثم إن كماله تعالى أجل الكمالات وإدراكه أقوى الإدراكات فوجب أن تكون لذاته أقوى اللذات ولذلك قالوا أجل مبتهج هو المبدأ الأول بذاته تعالى ( والجواب لا نسلم أن اللذة نفس الإدراك كما مر وإذا كان سبباً للذة فقد لا تكون ذاته قابلة للذة ووجود السبب لا يكفي لوجود المسبب (دون وجود القابل وإن سلم قبول ذاته لها (فلم) قلت إن إدراكنا مماثل لإدراكه في الحقيقة حتى يكون هو أيضاً سبباً للذة كإدراكنا ولو قدم هذا السؤال الثالث على الثاني لكان له وجه وجيه كما لا يخفى على ذي فطرة سليمة. قوله قالوا اللذة إدراك الملائم قيل الحق أنه مسامحة منهم وإلا فاللذة هو الابتهاج المترتب على ذلك، الإدراك لا نفسه كما يشهد به الوجدان . قوله : ( ولو قدم هذا السؤال إلخ) وجه الأولوية أن منع قابليته ينبغي أن يكون بعد تسليم الشركة في العلة لا بالعكس، واعلم أن كلام الشارح يشعر بأن السؤال ثلاثة وتلخيص الكلام يقتضي أن يكون اثنين لأن قوله وإذا كان سببا إلخ من تتمة منع كون اللذة نفس الإدراك . نعم هاهنا سؤال ثالث، وهو أنه لو كان متلذذاً بإدراك الملائم وجب أن يكون متألماً بإدراك المنافر كإثبات التثليث مثلاً لكنه باطل بالاتفاق، فليتأمل.
The Second Observatory - The Seventh Goal: That He, exalted, is not described with anything. And the philosophers affirmed it, saying: Pleasure is awareness of what is agreeable. So whoever is aware of a perfection in his essence delights in it, and that is self-evident — the intuition bears witness to it. Furthermore, His perfection, exalted, is the greatest of perfections and His awareness is the strongest of awarenesses, so it is necessary that His pleasure be the strongest of pleasures. Therefore they said: The most joyful is the First Principle by His own essence, exalted. And the answer: We do not accept that pleasure is identical to awareness, as has passed. And if it is a cause for pleasure, then His essence may not be receptive to pleasure, and the existence of the cause is insufficient for the existence of the effect without the existence of the receptive subject. And even if the receptivity of His essence is granted, why do you say that our awareness is similar to His awareness in reality so that it also becomes a cause for pleasure like our awareness? And if this third question were presented before the second, it would have a good justification, as is not hidden from one of sound nature. He said: They said pleasure is awareness of what is agreeable. It was said: The truth is that this is a concession from them, otherwise pleasure is the delight resulting from that awareness, not the awareness itself, as the intuition bears witness. He said: (And if this question were presented, etc.) The reason for priority is that denying its receptivity should be after conceding the sharing in the cause, not the reverse. Know that the speech of the commentator suggests that the questions are three, but the summary of the speech requires that they be two, because his saying 'and if it is a cause, etc.' is part of the denial that pleasure is identical to awareness. Yes, here there is a third question: that if He were experiencing pleasure from awareness of what is agreeable, it would be necessary that He be experiencing pain from awareness of what is disagreeable, like establishing the Trinity, for example, but that is false by consensus. So let it be pondered.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' argument that God experiences pleasure. The author first presents the philosophers' claim: pleasure is awareness of the agreeable; God's awareness of His perfect essence is the strongest, so His pleasure is the greatest. The refutation follows in three parts. First, pleasure is not identical to awareness—it is the delight that follows it, as our intuition confirms. Second, even if awareness were a cause, God's essence might not be receptive to pleasure; a cause is insufficient without a receptive subject. Third, even if He were receptive, our awareness is not analogous to His, so the argument by analogy fails. The author then notes a third question: if God experiences pleasure from awareness of the agreeable, He would necessarily experience pain from awareness of what displeases Him, like the doctrine of the Trinity, which is false. The passage emphasizes that the philosophers' concession about the definition of pleasure is inexact, and the proper ordering of questions clarifies the logical progression.

Key Terms
اللذة
الإدراك
الملائم
المنافر
الوجدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the philosophers' argument fails only on technical definitions, but it also leads to a contradiction with divine attributes.
Correction: The third question shows that the philosophers' view would force God to experience pain, which contradicts His perfection.

Checkpoint
What is the third question raised in this passage?
A) God's awareness is not analogous to ours.
B) If God feels pleasure from the agreeable, He must feel pain from the disagreeable.
C) The philosophers' definition of pleasure is wrong.
D) God's essence is not receptive to pleasure.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument leads to the absurd conclusion that God would experience pain, proving the original claim false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever imagined God feeling pain over your sins? How does this lesson's insistence on divine transcendence challenge that imagination? Will you consciously renounce such thoughts and instead focus on His mercy?
?
The philosophers assumed pleasure and pain are opposites. Do you ever treat God as if He is bound by human emotions? What would it mean for your worship to truly believe that God is beyond all such duality?
?
If God cannot feel pain, then His compassion is not sentimental but rooted in His will. How does this reshape your trust in Him when you suffer? Will you commit to praising Him even when your own emotions are painful?

Divine Attributes
beginner

Sectarian Positions on Divine Attributes
Introduces three main sectarian views on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main Islamic theological positions on whether God's attributes are additional to His essence or identical to it.

Core Idea
The Ash'aris affirm additional attributes; philosophers/Shi'a negate them; the Mu'tazila have a nuanced middle view.

This lesson begins the fourth observation on existential attributes. The author first presents the Ash'ari position, then the negation by philosophers and Shi'a, and finally a preview of the Mu'tazila detailed view. You will also encounter a useful gloss on the Shi'a application of the beautiful names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the three distinct sectarian positions mentioned.
The author uses a very compact style; reading aloud helps.
2
Identify the Ash'ari claim
Find the sentence beginning with 'ذهبت الأشاعرة' and note the list of attributes (knowledge, power, will, etc.). Understand the phrase 'زائدة على الذات'.
3
Contrast with philosophers/Shi'a
Read how philosophers and Shi'a negate additional attributes. Then note the internal Shi'a disagreement on applying names. The gloss criticizes those who withhold names despite Qur'anic usage.
4
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The passage previews a detailed Mu'tazila view. The gloss explains early Mu'tazila (affirmers of states, five states) and later Mu'tazila (identify attributes with essence).
This is a quick overview; details come later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع : في الصفات الوجودية ] ) في الصفات الوجودية وفيه مقاصد ) ثمانية . المقصد الأول : في إثبات الصفات ] ( في إثبات الصفات ( لله تعالى ( على وجه عام ) لا يختص بصفة دون أخرى، ذهبت الأشاعرة ومن تأسى بهم ) إلى أن له تعالى (صفات ) موجودة قديمة ( زائدة ) على ذاته ) فهو عالم بعلم قادر بقدرة مريد بإرادة وعلى هذا القياس فهو سميع يسمع بصير يبصر حي بحياة . وذهبت الفلاسفة والشيعة إلى نفيها ) أي نفي الصفات الزائدة على الذات فقالوا هو عالم الذات وقادر بالذات، وكذا سائر الصفات خلاف للشيعة في إطلاق الأسماء الحسنى عليه ) فمنهم من لم يطلق شيئاً منها عليه ومنهم من لم يجوز خلوه عنها، والمعتزلة لهم في الصفات تفصيل يأتي في مسألة ) مسألة من مباحثها ( احتجت ) الأشاعرة على ما ذهبوا إليه ( بوجوه ) ثلاثة ( الأول ما اعتمد عليه القدماء ) من الأشاعرة ( وهو قياس الغائب على الشاهد فإن العلة واحدة والشرط لا يختلف غائباً وشاهداً، ولا شك أن علة كون الشيء عالماً الشاهد هو العلم فكذا في الغائب وحد العالم هاهنا من قام به العلم فكذا حده هناك، وشرط صدق المشتق على واحد منا ثبوت أصله له فكذا شرطه فيمن غاب عنا
قوله: (فمنهم من لم يطلق شيئاً منها (عليه لا يخفى أن عدم الإطلاق مع وروده في القرآن المجيد والأحاديث الصحيحة من آثار الجهل ثم الأنسب أن يقول بدل قوله ومنهم من لم يجوز خلوه عنها ومنهم من أطلق كلاً منها عليه تعالى .
قوله : ( والمعتزلة لهم في الصفات تفصيل قال في حواشي التجريد مذهب قدماء المعتزلة أن الصفات ليست بموجودة ولا معدومة، وهؤلاء مثبتوا الأحوال والمشهور بإثباتها البهشمية وأنهم قد أثبتوا الله تعالى أحوالاً خمسة جملتها الألوهية المميزة لذاته تعالى عن غيره و مذهب المحدثين من المعتزلة موافقة الحكماء في نفي الصفات القديمة والقول بأنها عين
الذات .
من
قوله: (وشرط صدق المشتق على واحد منا ثبوت (أصله) فيه أن اللازم من هذا كون الصفات أموراً وراء الذات، وأما أنها أمور موجودة على ما هو المدعى في هذا المقام فلا يثبت بهذا والجواب بظهور أن العلم وكذا لقدرة مثلا وغيرهما ليس من الأمور الاعتبارية العقلية التي لا
The fourth observation: on the existential attributes. And it contains eight purposes. The first purpose: on the proof of attributes, for Allah Most High, in a general way, not specific to any attribute. The Ash'aris and those who follow them held that He Most High has attributes, existent, eternal, additional to His essence. Thus He is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, willing by will, and on this analogy: He is hearing by hearing, seeing by sight, living by life. The philosophers and the Shi'a held to their negation—that is, the negation of attributes additional to the essence. They said: He is knowing by essence, powerful by essence, and so on for the rest of the attributes. There is disagreement among the Shi'a regarding the application of the beautiful names to Him: some do not apply any of them to Him, and some do not permit His being devoid of them. The Mu'tazila have a detailed position on attributes which will come in a question. The Ash'aris argued for their position with three aspects. The first is what the early Ash'aris relied upon: analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid). For the cause is one, and the condition does not differ between unseen and witnessed. There is no doubt that the cause of a thing's being knowing in the witnessed is knowledge; similarly in the unseen. The definition of 'knowing' here is 'one in whom knowledge subsists'; similarly its definition there. And the condition for the truth of the derived term (mushtaqq) applied to one of us is the subsistence of its source in him; similarly its condition for the one absent from us. His statement: 'Some of them do not apply any of them to Him'—it is not hidden that non-application despite its occurrence in the Noble Qur'an and authentic hadiths is from the effects of ignorance. Then it is more appropriate that he say instead of his statement 'and some of them do not permit His being devoid of them' and 'some of them apply all of them to Him Most High.' His statement: 'And the Mu'tazila have a detailed position on attributes.' He said in the glosses on al-Tajrid: The school of the early Mu'tazila is that the attributes are neither existent nor non-existent. These are the affirmers of states (ahwal), and the most famous among them for affirming states is al-Bahshamiyya, and they affirmed for Allah Most High five states: the totality of which is divinity, distinguishing His essence from others. And the school of the later Mu'tazila is agreement with the philosophers in negating eternal attributes and saying they are identical to the essence. His statement: 'And the condition for the truth of the derived term applied to one of us is the subsistence of its source in him'—the implication of this is that the attributes are things beyond the essence. But that they are existent things, as is claimed in this context, is not proven by this. The answer: by the appearance that knowledge, and similarly power for example, and others, are not among the intellectual considerations that are not...
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the fourth observation (al-marsad al-rabi') on existential attributes (al-sifat al-wujudiyya) and the first of its eight purposes: the general proof of attributes. The author first states the Ash'ari position: God has attributes that are existent, eternal, and additional (za'ida) to the essence—He is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, etc. Then he contrasts the philosophers and Shi'a who negate additional attributes, asserting knowledge by essence, etc., though Shi'a differ on applying the names. The Mu'tazila have a detailed view to be discussed later. The Ash'aris present three proofs; the first, relied upon by early Ash'aris, is qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy of the unseen from the witnessed). The reasoning: the cause ('illa) of being knowing in the witnessed is knowledge; the same applies to the unseen. The definition of 'knowing' is one in whom knowledge subsists; similarly for the unseen. The condition for the truth of the derived term (mushtaqq) like 'knowing' is the subsistence of its source (knowledge) in the subject. So for God, the same condition holds, requiring knowledge as an additional attribute. The passage then includes marginal glosses: one criticizes the Shi'a who refuse to apply any names despite Qur'anic usage, suggesting a better classification; another explains the Mu'tazila division: early ones (affirmers of states, ahwal) like al-Bahshamiyya, who posit five states including divinity, and later ones who agree with the philosophers in identifying attributes with essence. A final gloss on the proof's condition notes that the argument shows attributes are beyond the essence (amurun wara'a al-dhat) but not necessarily that they are existent—the answer given is that knowledge and power are not merely intellectual considerations, implying their real existence, though the text cuts off.

Key Terms
الصفات الوجودية
الصفات الزائدة
الأحوال
البهشمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Shi'a uniformly deny application of the beautiful names to God.
Correction: The Shi'a are divided: some do not apply any names, some do not permit their absence, and the gloss suggests a third group that applies all names.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris, how are God's attributes related to His essence?
A) They are identical to the essence.
B) They are neither existent nor non-existent.
C) They are additional (za'ida) to the essence and existent.
D) They are only mental considerations.

Key Takeaway
The main early Islamic positions on divine attributes are: Ash'ari affirmation of additional attributes, philosophers/Shi'a negation, and Mu'tazila affirmation of states or identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His names, like 'al-Alim' (the All-Knowing), do you think of His knowledge as something added to His essence, or do you feel it is simply a description of who He is? Which of the three positions (Ash'ari, philosopher/Shi'a, or Mu'tazila) does your instinct lean toward, and how does that affect the sincerity of your invocation?
?
The Mu'tazila introduced 'states' such as 'divinity' to explain God's attributes. When you reflect on the meaning of 'Allah' as the God, do you sense a distinct 'state' of lordship that permeates your awareness? How might this shape your consciousness of His presence in your daily life?
?
If you were to defend your understanding of God's attributes before a scholar from each of these three schools, what would you say? Would your explanation be grounded in the texts you have studied, or in a personal conviction you have never fully examined? What is at stake for your faith if your view is wrong?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
The First Proof: Qiyas al-Gha'ib 'ala al-Shahid
Presents the first Ash'ari proof for additional attributes: qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof from analogy (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid) for the existence of attributes as real entities additional to the divine essence.

Core Idea
If 'knowing' requires knowledge in creatures, then the same must apply to God because the cause and condition are universal.

The Ash'aris advance three proofs for attributes; this lesson focuses on the first, which relies on analogical reasoning from the witnessed world to the unseen divine. The argument hinges on the condition for applying derived terms (mushtaqqat) like 'knowing' and 'powerful'. A gloss at the end addresses a potential objection about the reality of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic for the first proof (from 'احتجت الأشاعرة' to the end of the passage). Follow the translation closely.
2
Identify the analogical structure
Notice the steps: (1) cause of being 'knowing' in creatures is knowledge, (2) same cause applies to God, (3) definition of 'knowing' is one in whom knowledge subsists, (4) condition for derived term is subsistence of its source. This proves attributes are things beyond the essence.
3
Analyze the condition
The proof uses the principle: for a derived term like 'knowing' to be true of someone, the source (knowledge) must subsist in that person. This holds for creatures and for God.
4
Consider the gloss
The gloss notes that the argument proves attributes are 'beyond the essence' but not necessarily that they are existent. The answer is that knowledge is not a mere mental consideration, implying real existence, but the text cuts off.
This shows a deeper discussion about the nature of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الذات .

قوله: (وشرط صدق المشتق على واحد منا ثبوت (أصله) فيه أن اللازم من هذا كون الصفات أموراً وراء الذات، وأما أنها أمور موجودة على ما هو المدعى في هذا المقام فلا يثبت بهذا والجواب بظهور أن العلم وكذا لقدرة مثلا وغيرهما ليس من الأمور الاعتبارية العقلية التي لا
The fourth observation: on the existential attributes. And it contains eight purposes. The first purpose: on the proof of attributes, for Allah Most High, in a general way, not specific to any attribute. The Ash'aris and those who follow them held that He Most High has attributes, existent, eternal, additional to His essence. Thus He is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, willing by will, and on this analogy: He is hearing by hearing, seeing by sight, living by life. The philosophers and the Shi'a held to their negation—that is, the negation of attributes additional to the essence. They said: He is knowing by essence, powerful by essence, and so on for the rest of the attributes. There is disagreement among the Shi'a regarding the application of the beautiful names to Him: some do not apply any of them to Him, and some do not permit His being devoid of them. The Mu'tazila have a detailed position on attributes which will come in a question. The Ash'aris argued for their position with three aspects. The first is what the early Ash'aris relied upon: analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid). For the cause is one, and the condition does not differ between unseen and witnessed. There is no doubt that the cause of a thing's being knowing in the witnessed is knowledge; similarly in the unseen. The definition of 'knowing' here is 'one in whom knowledge subsists'; similarly its definition there. And the condition for the truth of the derived term (mushtaqq) applied to one of us is the subsistence of its source in him; similarly its condition for the one absent from us. His statement: 'Some of them do not apply any of them to Him'—it is not hidden that non-application despite its occurrence in the Noble Qur'an and authentic hadiths is from the effects of ignorance. Then it is more appropriate that he say instead of his statement 'and some of them do not permit His being devoid of them' and 'some of them apply all of them to Him Most High.' His statement: 'And the Mu'tazila have a detailed position on attributes.' He said in the glosses on al-Tajrid: The school of the early Mu'tazila is that the attributes are neither existent nor non-existent. These are the affirmers of states (ahwal), and the most famous among them for affirming states is al-Bahshamiyya, and they affirmed for Allah Most High five states: the totality of which is divinity, distinguishing His essence from others. And the school of the later Mu'tazila is agreement with the philosophers in negating eternal attributes and saying they are identical to the essence. His statement: 'And the condition for the truth of the derived term applied to one of us is the subsistence of its source in him'—the implication of this is that the attributes are things beyond the essence. But that they are existent things, as is claimed in this context, is not proven by this. The answer: by the appearance that knowledge, and similarly power for example, and others, are not among the intellectual considerations that are not...
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the fourth observation (al-marsad al-rabi') on existential attributes (al-sifat al-wujudiyya) and the first of its eight purposes: the general proof of attributes. The author first states the Ash'ari position: God has attributes that are existent, eternal, and additional (za'ida) to the essence—He is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, etc. Then he contrasts the philosophers and Shi'a who negate additional attributes, asserting knowledge by essence, etc., though Shi'a differ on applying the names. The Mu'tazila have a detailed view to be discussed later. The Ash'aris present three proofs; the first, relied upon by early Ash'aris, is qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy of the unseen from the witnessed). The reasoning: the cause ('illa) of being knowing in the witnessed is knowledge; the same applies to the unseen. The definition of 'knowing' is one in whom knowledge subsists; similarly for the unseen. The condition for the truth of the derived term (mushtaqq) like 'knowing' is the subsistence of its source (knowledge) in the subject. So for God, the same condition holds, requiring knowledge as an additional attribute. The passage then includes marginal glosses: one criticizes the Shi'a who refuse to apply any names despite Qur'anic usage, suggesting a better classification; another explains the Mu'tazila division: early ones (affirmers of states, ahwal) like al-Bahshamiyya, who posit five states including divinity, and later ones who agree with the philosophers in identifying attributes with essence. A final gloss on the proof's condition notes that the argument shows attributes are beyond the essence (amurun wara'a al-dhat) but not necessarily that they are existent—the answer given is that knowledge and power are not merely intellectual considerations, implying their real existence, though the text cuts off.

Key Terms

المشتق
ثبوت أصله
العلة

C) That knowledge subsists in that person.
D) That the person is a rational being.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris prove additional attributes by arguing that the condition for calling creatures 'knowing' (subsistence of knowledge) must also apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If the same logical condition for being 'knowing' applies to God as to creatures, then God's knowledge is as real as your own. Do you live each day with the awareness that God knows not only your actions but your innermost thoughts? In what moment today did you act as if His knowledge were distant?
?
The proof shows that attributes like knowledge and power are not mere concepts but real entities subsisting in God. How would your relationship with God change if you truly believed that His power is just as real and present as the power you experience in yourself? Commit to one specific change in your daily life that reflects this belief.

You accept that God is 'Knowing' and 'Powerful'. But if these attributes are real, then you are always under His observation. What sin have you committed recently that you would not have done if you had fully felt His knowledge and power? Will you repent now and resolve to remember His attributes?

The First Ash'ari Proof: Qiyas al-Gha'ib 'ala al-Shahid and Its Refutation
Presents and refutes the first Ash'ari proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the Ash'ari analogy from the witnessed to the unseen and the key refutations that undermine its validity.

Core Idea
Analogy fails when the properties of compared things differ.

The Ash'aris presented three proofs for their view on divine attributes; the first is the analogy from the witnessed to the unseen. This passage presents that analogy and then offers a multi-pronged refutation. You will see how the argument's own assumptions lead to its collapse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note how the Ash'ari analogy is presented and then immediately refuted.
The refutation has three distinct points; try to identify each.
2
Understand the analogy
The Ash'aris argue that the cause (illa) of a thing's being knowing is knowledge, and this cause is the same in both the witnessed and the unseen. Grasp this reasoning.
The definition of 'knowing' is one in whom knowledge subsists.
3
Analyze the refutation
The refutation first reminds of known weakness, then gives concrete examples where implications differ (power and will), and finally denies that root attributes are established in the witnessed.
These points together show the analogy collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجت الأشاعرة على ما ذهبوا إليه بوجوه ثلاثة الأول ما اعتمد عليه القدماء من الأشاعرة وهو قياس الغائب على الشاهد فإن العلة واحدة والشرط لا يختلف غائباً وشاهداً، ولا شك أن علة كون الشيء عالماً الشاهد هو العلم فكذا في الغائب وحد العالم هاهنا من قام به العلم فكذا حده هناك، وشرط صدق المشتق على واحد منا ثبوت أصله له فكذا شرطه فيمن غاب عنا وقد عرفت ضعفه في المرصد الأخير من الموقف الأول كيف والخصم أي القائس كما وقع في كلام الآمدي قائل ومعترف باختلاف مقتضى الصفات شاهداً وغائباً، فإن القدرة في الشاهد لا يتصور فيها الإيجاد بخلافها في الغائب والإرادة فيه لا تخصص بخلاف إرادة الغائب وكذا الحال في باقي الصفات فإذا وجد في أحدهما لم يوجد في الآخر فلا يصح القياس أصلاً، وقد يمنع ثبوتها أي ثبوت العلم والقدرة والإرادة ونظائرها في الشاهد بل الثابت فيه بيقين هو العالمية والقادرية والمريدية لا ما مشتقة منها فيضمحل القياس بالكلية
The Ash'aris argued for what they held by three proofs. The first is that which the early Ash'aris relied upon, namely the analogy of the unseen from the witnessed. For the cause (illa) is one and the condition does not differ between the unseen and the witnessed. There is no doubt that the cause of a thing's being knowing in the witnessed is knowledge, so likewise in the unseen. The definition of the knowing one here is one in whom knowledge subsists, so likewise its definition there. The condition for the truth of the derived term (mushtaqq) upon one of us is the subsistence of its root (asl) in him, so likewise its condition in one who is absent from us. And you have already known its weakness in the last observatory of the first standpoint. How [is it valid] while the opponent—i.e., the analogizer, as occurs in the speech of al-Amidi—is saying and admitting that the implications of the attributes differ between the witnessed and the unseen? For power in the witnessed cannot be conceived to create (ijad), unlike power in the unseen; and will in the witnessed does not specify (takhsis), unlike the will in the unseen; and similarly the case for the rest of the attributes. So if it is found in one, it is not found in the other, so the analogy is not valid at all. And it may be denied that they—i.e., knowledge, power, will and their likes—are established in the witnessed. Rather, what is established in it with certainty is being knowing ('alimiyya), being powerful (qadiriyya), and being willing (muridiyya), not the derived terms from them, so the analogy collapses entirely.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-refutation unit. It begins by presenting the first Ash'ari proof: the analogy from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib). The Ash'aris argue that the cause (illa) of being knowing is knowledge, and this cause is the same in both realms. The definition of 'knowing' is one in whom knowledge subsists, and the condition for applying a derived term (mushtaqq) like 'knowing' is that its root (asl) subsists. So by analogy, God must have knowledge subsisting in Him. Then the author refutes this proof in two ways. First, he notes that its weakness was already shown. Second, he points out that the opponent (the analogizer himself) admits that the implications (muqtadayat) of attributes differ between the witnessed and the unseen. For example, power in the witnessed cannot create, whereas in the unseen it can; will in the witnessed does not specify, whereas in the unseen it does. Hence the analogy is invalid because the attributes have different properties. Third, he argues that one may even deny that knowledge, power, and will are established in the witnessed at all; rather, only the states of being knowing, being powerful, and being willing are certain, not the derived terms. This would also destroy the analogy. Thus the first proof is refuted.

إرادة
إيجاد
تخصص
عالمية
قادرية
مريدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Analogy (qiyas) from the witnessed to the unseen is always valid for establishing attributes.
Correction: The passage shows that analogy fails when the implications of attributes differ between the two realms, as the opponent himself admits.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection against the Ash'ari analogy from the witnessed to the unseen?
A) The cause is not known.
B) The opponent admits that the implications of attributes differ between the witnessed and the unseen.
C) The definition of 'knowing' is incorrect.
D) The analogy is forbidden by revelation.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari analogy from the witnessed to the unseen is invalid because the implications of attributes differ between the two realms, and the root attributes may not be established in the witnessed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you ever find yourself unconsciously limiting His power or will to what you have experienced in human capabilities? How can you actively remind yourself that His attributes are fundamentally different in their implications?
?
This passage shows that even a seemingly logical analogy can be deeply flawed if the properties of the compared things differ. Are there any analogies you hold about God that you have not critically examined? What would it cost you to let go of them and submit solely to revelation?
?
The Ash'aris built their proof on the assumption that the cause of an attribute is the same in God and humans. Where in your own life do you assume that God operates according to the same rules as you? How will you train yourself to recognize the radical otherness of His attributes?

Second Ash'ari Proof: The Tautology Argument
Second Ash'ari proof with defense against objectors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Ash'ari proof that divine attributes are not identical to the essence, and how the Ash'aris defend the objective reality of these attributes against relational or mentalist objections.

Core Idea
If attributes were the essence, saying 'God is knowing' would be a useless tautology.

This passage presents a powerful logical argument: predication of attributes must be informative. The Ash'ari then defends the objective reality of these attributes against objections that they are merely mental considerations or pure relations. We'll walk through the proof, its supporting claim about distinction, and the rebuttal of the 'hal' objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the compressed style; the argument is dense.
2
Identify the main tautology argument
The proof states that if the concept of being knowing, living, powerful were the essence itself, then predicating them of God would be like saying 'a thing is itself' – uninformative. Since we know these predications are meaningful, the attributes must be additional.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the defense of distinct existence
The passage argues that attributes have existence in concrete individuals (a'yan); otherwise they would not be distinct from each other, because non-existents are indistinguishable.
This reinforces that attributes are real and distinct.
4
Analyze the objection and reply
An objection says there is no 'state' (hal) that determines their existence, implying they are mental. The reply: most theologians consider knowledge a pure relation, and relations are mental considerations. However, claiming God's knowledge is a real meaning without proof is extremely difficult.
The Ash'ari position is that attributes are real, but the strongest defense is the tautology argument itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني لو كان مفهوم كونه عالماً حياً قادراً نفس ذاته لم يفد حملها على ذاته وكان قولنا على طريقة الإخبار الله الواجب أو العالم أو القادر أو الحي إلى سائر الصفات بمثابة حمل الشيء على نفسه واللازم باطل، لأن هي وجود لها في الأعيان وإلا لم يتمايز لأن المعدومات غير متمايزة كما سبق، وإذ لا حال تعين وجودها يدفعه أن جمهور المتكلمين ذهبوا إلى أن العلم إضافة محضة ولا شك أن الإضافة عندهم من الاعتبارات العقلية، ودعوى أن علم الله تعالى من المعاني الحقيقة وإن لم يكن علم الممكن كذلك دون إثباتها خرط القتاد
The second proof: If the concept of His being knowing, living, powerful were the very essence, then predicating them of the essence would not be informative, and our statement, in the manner of report, 'God is the Necessary', or 'the Knowing', or 'the Powerful', or 'the Living', and the rest of the attributes, would be like predicating a thing of itself. And the consequent is false. Because they have existence in concrete individuals; otherwise they would not be distinct, since non-existents are not distinct as already stated. And since there is no state (hal) that determines their existence—an objection repelled by the fact that the majority of theologians held that knowledge is a pure relation (idafa), and there is no doubt that relation according to them is among mental considerations. And the claim that the knowledge of God, exalted, is among real meanings, even if the knowledge of a possible is not like that, without proving it, is an impossible task.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a proof and its defense. It presents the second Ash'ari argument: if the concept of being knowing, living, and powerful were identical to the essence itself, then predicating these terms of the essence would be like saying 'a thing is itself'—a tautology that provides no information. Since we know that such predications are meaningful (e.g., 'God is knowing'), the antecedent must be false; hence the attributes are additional to the essence. The text then defends the reality of these attributes: they have existence in concrete individuals (a'yan), otherwise they would not be distinct from each other, because non-existents are not distinct. Then it addresses an objection: if someone says there is no state (hal) that determines their existence (i.e., attributes are mere mental considerations), the author replies that most theologians hold that knowledge is a pure relation (idafa) and relations are mental considerations. But the claim that God's knowledge is a real meaning (ma'na haqiqi) even if human knowledge is not, without proving it, is an extremely difficult undertaking (kharṭ al-qatād). In other words, the Ash'ari position that attributes are real and additional is defended against objections that they are merely relational or mental.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari tautology argument proves attributes are merely mental or relational.
Correction: Actually, the argument proves they are additional to the essence; the defense against the 'hal' objection shows that even if some consider knowledge a relation, the Ash'ari aim is to affirm real attributes, though proving God's knowledge as a real meaning is difficult.

Checkpoint

A) God would not be knowing.
B) Predication would be a tautology like 'a thing is itself'.
C) Knowledge would be created.
D) There would be no distinction between attributes.

Key Takeaway
The second Ash'ari proof shows that if attributes were identical to the essence, predication would be meaningless; thus attributes are additional real entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage warns that claiming God's knowledge is a 'real meaning' without proof is 'an impossible task.' Are there theological beliefs you hold simply because you were taught them, without ever demanding evidence? Are you willing to examine each belief and either find its proof or admit your uncertainty?

The Difference in the Implications of Attributes Invalidates Qiyas
Al-Jurjani presents the first argument against qiyas: difference in implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the difference in the implications of attributes between the witnessed and the unseen invalidates the analogy used to prove divine attributes.

Core Idea
What holds in the witnessed does not necessarily hold in the unseen.

Al-Jurjani argues that the Ash'arite opponent admits that the implications of attributes differ between the witnessed and the unseen. For example, power in the witnessed does not include creation, while divine power does. Therefore, any analogy from the witnessed to the unseen is flawed. Let's examine this argument in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation to grasp Al-Jurjani's line of reasoning.
Pay attention to the examples of power and will.
2
Identify the key difference
Notice that Al-Jurjani points out that power in the witnessed does not include bringing into existence, while divine power does. Similarly, will in the witnessed does not specify, unlike divine will.
This shows a fundamental difference in the implications of attributes.
3
Understand why this breaks the analogy
Because the analogy assumes similarity, but here there is a clear difference, so the analogy cannot hold.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that since one is found in one but not the other, the analogy is not valid at all.
This is a strong refutation of the use of qiyas in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الأول : في إثبات الصفات
كيف
وقس على ذلك سائر الصفات ) وقد عرفت ضعفه ) في المرضد الأخير من الموقف الأول ( كيف والخصم أي القائس كما وقع في كلام الآمدي (قائل) ومعترف ( باختلاف مقتضي الصفات شاهداً وغائباً)، فإن القدرة في الشاهد لا يتصور فيها الإيجاد بخلافها في الغائب والإرادة فيه لا تخصص بخلاف إرادة الغائب وكذا الحال في باقي الصفات فإذا وجد في أحدهما لم يوجد في الآخر فلا يصح القياس أصلاً، ( وقد يمنع ثبوتها أي ثبوت العلم والقدرة والإرادة ونظائرها ( في الشاهد بل الثابت فيه ) بيقين هو ( العالمية والقادرية والمريدية) لا ما مشتقة منها فيضمحل القياس بالكلية
The fourth observatory - the first goal: on establishing the attributes. How? And measure by that the rest of the attributes – and you have known its weakness – in the last observatory of the first standpoint. How, while the opponent, i.e., the one making the analogy as occurs in the speech of al-Amidi, says and admits the difference in the implications of the attributes in the witnessed and the unseen. For power in the witnessed cannot be imagined to bring into existence, unlike it in the unseen, and will in it does not specify, unlike the will of the unseen, and likewise the case for the rest of the attributes. So if one is found in one of them, it is not found in the other, so the analogy is not valid at all. And it may be denied that they are established – i.e., the establishment of knowledge, power, will and their likes – in the witnessed; rather, what is established in it with certainty is 'being knowing', 'being powerful', and 'being willing', not what is derived from them. Thus the analogy collapses entirely.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against using analogy (qiyas) from the witnessed (shāhid) to the unseen (ghāʼib) to prove the existence of divine attributes. The commentator first cites the matn: 'How?' referring to a previous argument, then states that one should not measure other attributes by the same standard, as its weakness was shown earlier. The opponent (here the Ashʻarites) concedes that the implications of attributes differ between the witnessed and the unseen. For example, power in the witnessed does not include creation, whereas divine power does; will in the witnessed does not specify (i.e., does not necessitate creation of one of two possibilities), unlike divine will. Thus, what holds in one does not hold in the other, invalidating the analogy. Furthermore, even in the witnessed, it is not certain that attributes like knowledge, power, and will exist as real entities; rather, only the derivative names (such as 'knowing', 'powerful', 'willing') are certain. This undermines the analogy completely.

الإرادة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if an attribute exists in the witnessed, it must exist in the unseen in the same way.
Correction: The passage shows that the implications differ, so the analogy fails.

Checkpoint

A) Power in the witnessed includes creation, while divine power does not.
B) Power in the witnessed does not include creation, while divine power does.
C) Both include creation.
D) Neither includes creation.

Key Takeaway
The difference in the implications of attributes between the witnessed and the unseen destroys the foundation of the analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Denying the Reality of Attributes in the Witnessed
Al-Jurjani denies the establishment of real attributes in the witnessed, only the derived names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against qiyas: that the attributes in the witnessed are not established as real entities, only the derived names.

Core Idea
Only the names 'knowing,' 'powerful,' and 'willing' are certain, not the attributes themselves.

Al-Jurjani presents a further objection: even if we accept the analogy, it is not certain that attributes like knowledge, power, and will exist in the witnessed as real entities. What is certain are only the derived names. This undermines the analogy entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the second argument.
Notice the mention of 'being knowing', 'being powerful', 'being willing'.
2
Distinguish between attribute and derived name
The passage says that in the witnessed, only the derived names (like 'knowing') are established with certainty, not the attributes themselves (like 'knowledge').
This is a subtle but important distinction.
3
Understand the consequence
If the real attributes are not established in the witnessed, then the analogy cannot proceed because the premises are uncertain.
4
Reflect on the collapse of the analogy
The passage concludes that this argument causes the analogy to collapse entirely.
This is a strong rebuttal.

A) Knowledge, power, and will as real attributes.
B) The derivative names: 'being knowing', 'being powerful', 'being willing'.
C) Both the attributes and the derived names.
D) Neither the attributes nor the names.

Key Takeaway
Since only the derived names are certain in the witnessed, any analogy to prove real attributes in the unseen is invalid.

?

?
This passage shows that a commonly used argument for God's attributes is flawed. How often do you rely on your own reasoning rather than revelation to define Allah? What would it take to submit fully to what He has told us?

Second Argument: Attributes Cannot Be Identical to Essence
Al-Jurjani's second argument for the addition of attributes to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's second argument for the addition of attributes to the essence and its limitation to conceptual addition.

Core Idea
Predicating an attribute gives new information, so it cannot be identical to the essence.

Al-Jurjani presents a logical argument: if 'knowing' were the same as 'essence', saying 'God is knowing' would be like saying 'essence is essence', which tells us nothing. Since it does tell us something, the attribute must be additional. However, this only proves conceptual addition, which is not the real point of contention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Jurjani sets up the conditional and its consequent.
The passage uses a conditional structure: if P then Q, Q is false, so not P.
2
Identify the premise and the consequent
The premise is that if the concept of the attribute were identical to the essence, then predication would be meaningless. The consequent is that such predication is meaningful. Ask yourself whether you agree.
3
Understand the argument's structure
The argument is: If A = essence, then predication 'God is A' is tautological. But predication is not tautological (it gives benefit). Therefore A ≠ essence. This establishes addition of the concept of A to the concept of essence.
The addition is conceptual, not necessarily real.
4
Recognize the limitation
The commentator notes that this only proves conceptual addition. The real debate is whether the attribute has an extra existence in reality. Ask yourself why this distinction matters.
This connects to the broader discussion on divine attributes.

The second face: If the concept of His being knowing, living, powerful were the same as His essence, then predicating them of His essence would not be beneficial, and our saying in the manner of informing: 'the Necessary God' or 'the Knowing' or 'the Powerful' or 'the Living' and the rest of the attributes would be like predicating a thing of itself, and the consequent is false. Because predicating these attributes yields a valid benefit, unlike our saying 'His essence is His essence'. And if it is false that it is the same as the essence, and there is no room for partiality at all, then the addition to the essence is established. And in it there is consideration: for it only proves the addition of this concept – i.e., the concept of 'knowing' and 'powerful' and their likes – to the concept of the essence, and there is no dispute about that. As for the addition of what this concept applies to (the real existence) over the reality of the essence, this proof does not show it. But if both (the concept of the attribute and the concept of the essence) were conceived in their realities and it were possible to predicate one (the attribute) of the essence without predicating the other (the essence) of it, then the desired result—the attribute being additional to the essence—would be attained. However, conception that reaches the inner reality of both is impossible. [Then follow the commentary paragraphs.]
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-wajh al-thānī) for the attributes being additional to the essence. It begins by stating the premise: if the concept of being knowing, living, powerful were identical to the essence, then predicating these descriptions would be as useless as saying 'the essence is the essence'. Since these predications do give useful information (e.g., 'God is knowing' tells us something new), the consequent (that they are identical) is false. Therefore, the attributes must be additional to the essence. However, a critical observation follows: this argument only establishes that the concept (mafhūm) of the attribute is additional to the concept of the essence—a conceptual distinction, not a real distinction in existence. The real point of contention is whether the attribute has an extra existence in reality, not just in concept. The commentator then notes that if one could conceive both the essence and the attribute in their true realities, and see that they can be predicated of each other in a certain way, the real addition would be proven, but such conception is unattainable. The subsequent 'قوله' lines are marginal annotations further clarifying points about the earlier arguments, such as the question of analogy and the meaning of 'takhṣīṣ'.

The Third Argument: Attributes Cannot Be Identical to the Essence
Al-Jurjani presents the third argument and clarifies its limited scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument (al-wajh al-thālith) for the attributes being additional to the essence, including its logical structure and the crucial distinction between conceptual and real distinction.

Core Idea
If two attributes were identical to the essence, they'd be identical to each other—impossible.

This lesson explores a powerful reductio argument: if knowledge and power were each identical to the divine essence, they would become identical to each other, which is self-evidently false. But does this argument prove that attributes are really distinct from the essence, or only conceptually distinct? Al-Jurjani clarifies the exact scope of this proof and warns against a common confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the conditional statement and its conclusion. Then read the translation to ensure you grasp the logical flow.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: from the opponents' premise, an absurd consequence follows.
2
Identify the key elements
Spot the conditional (law…la…), the conclusion (knowledge = power), and the claim that this is necessarily false. Also note that the same applies to all attributes.
The phrase 'ضروري البطلان' means 'necessarily false'.
3
Understand the limitation
Al-Jurjani points out that this argument only demonstrates a conceptual distinction (taghāyur al-mafhūm), not a real distinction in existence (taghāyur al-ḥaqīqa). The real point of dispute is the latter.
This is the key nuance: the opponent denies real distinction, but the argument only disproves identity at the conceptual level.
4
Reflect on the root problem
The passage states that the source of this confusion is failing to distinguish between the concept (mafhūm) of a thing and its reality (ḥaqīqa). Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter for the attributes debate?
Concepts can be distinct even if the realities are identical (e.g., 'morning star' and 'evening star').
5
Consider the objection and response
The passage includes an anticipated objection: how can an attribute be identical to the essence if common sense sees them as distinct? The response is implied: the problem arises from using 'attribute' in the philosophical sense. Reflect on how the theological definition differs.
This connects to the broader discussion of essential attributes (ṣifāt nafsiyya).

The third face: If knowledge were the same as the essence, and power also the same as the essence as they claim, then knowledge would be the same as power, and the concept from knowledge and power would be one thing, and this is necessarily false. Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (al-wajh al-thālith) for the attributes being additional to the essence. It begins by stating: if knowledge is identical to the essence, and power is also identical to the essence (as claimed by those who deny the attributes), then knowledge and power would be identical to each other—a necessarily false conclusion. The same applies to all attributes claimed to be identical to the essence. The commentator then notes that this argument is of the same type as the previous one (the second face), meaning it only proves a conceptual distinction (taghāyur al-mafhūm) between knowledge and power and between each and the essence, not a real distinction in existence (taghāyur al-ḥaqīqa). The real point of dispute is the latter, not the former. The root problem of both arguments is failing to distinguish between the concept (mafhūm) of a thing and its reality (ḥaqīqa). The passage then anticipates an objection: how can an attribute be conceived as identical to the essence when common sense sees them as distinct? The commentator replies that this objection arises from using the term 'attribute' in the philosophical sense (meaning a real accident) rather than the theological sense, where attribute includes essential properties (dhātiyyāt). The following 'قوله' lines elaborate on specific points: the correct phrasing of the tautology argument, the need to speak of concepts like 'knowledge' rather than 'knowing', and the possibility of conceiving essences without needing to stipulate predication.

The Third Argument: Mutual Identity of Attributes
Presents the third argument against attributes being identical with essence and its objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against attributes being identical with the essence, why it fails, and the crucial distinction between concept and reality.

Core Idea
Arguments based on conceptual identity miss the real question: are the realities distinct?

This lesson presents the third argument used by those who deny that attributes are distinct from the essence. The argument claims that if two attributes like knowledge and power are both identical with the essence, they would be identical with each other, which is absurd. However, as the author explains, this argument only shows a conceptual difference, not a real one. Understanding the distinction between concept and reality is key to resolving this dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for overall meaning, then the translation. Notice the logical flow.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the argument's logical structure
The argument uses the transitivity of identity: if A = B and C = B, then A = C. Here, knowledge = essence and power = essence, so knowledge = power, which is false. Therefore, attributes cannot be identical with essence.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Identify the objection
The author points out that the argument only proves that the concepts of knowledge and power are different, not that their realities are distinct. The real dispute is about realities, not concepts.
The objection is the same as for the first argument.
4
Reflect on the distinction between concept and reality
Consider why this distinction matters. The failure to distinguish between concept (mafhūm) and reality (ḥaqīqah) leads to invalid arguments.
This is a key epistemological point in kalām.

Source Text

intermediate

How Can an Attribute Be Identical with the Essence?
Explains the philosophers' view that the essence itself produces the effects of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' position that God's essence itself performs the functions of attributes, and that the distinction between essence and attributes is only conceptual, not real.

Core Idea
God's essence alone suffices; attributes are just conceptual distinctions.

This passage addresses a natural question: if attributes are distinct from essence in our experience, how can they be identical in God? The answer clarifies that the philosophers do not assert literal identity but that God's essence produces the effects of attributes, making them one in reality while distinct in our minds.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and answer carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a hypothetical question followed by a detailed answer.
2
Understand the objection
The questioner argues that attributes seem distinct from essence in all beings, so claiming identity is absurd. Recognize why this objection arises from ordinary experience.
3
Grasp the answer's key claim
The answer explains that the philosophers do not mean literal identity. Rather, God's essence alone produces the effects that we associate with attributes (e.g., knowledge, power).
This is the core of the lesson.
4
Examine the examples
The example of human knowledge vs. God's knowledge: humans need an extra attribute, God does not. Similarly for power. See how the examples illustrate the point.
5
Realize the conclusion
The passage concludes that essence and attributes are united in reality but distinct in consideration (concept). This effectively negates real attributes while affirming their results from the essence.
This distinguishes the philosophers from both the Ash'aris and the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: كيف يتصور كون صفة الشيء عين حقيقته مع أن كل واحد من الموصوف والصفة يشهد بمغايرته لصاحبه وهل هذا إلا كلام مخيل لا يمكن أن يصدق به كما في سائر القضايا المخيلة التي يمتنع التصديق بها فلا حاجة بنا إلى الاستدلال على بطلانه. قلت: ليس معنى ما ذكروه أن هناك ذاتا وله صفة وهما متحدان حقيقة كما تخيلته بل معناه أن ذاته تعالى يترتب عليه ما يترتب على ذات وصفة معاً، مثلاً ذاتك ليست كافية في انكشاف الأشياء عليك بل تحتاج في ذلك إلى صفة العلم التي تقوم بك بخلاف ذاته تعالى فإنه لا يحتاج في انكشاف الأشياء وظهورها عليه إلى صفة تقوم به، بل المفهومات بأسرها منكشفة عليه لأجل ذاته تعالى فذاته بهذا الاعتبار حقيقة العلم وكذا الحال في القدرة فإن ذاته تعالى مؤثرة بذاتها لا بصفة زائدة عليها كما في ذواتنا، فهي بهذا الاعتبار حقيقة القدرة وعلى هذا تكون الذات والصفات متحدة في الحقيقة متغايرة بالاعتبار والمفهوم، ومرجعه إذا حقق إلى نفي الصفات مع حصول نتائجها وثمراتها من الذات وحدها.
If you say: How can one conceive that an attribute of a thing is identical with its essence, when each of the described and the attribute testifies to its distinction from the other? Is this not merely imaginary speech that cannot be believed, like other imaginary propositions whose truth is impossible, so we have no need to prove its falsehood? The answer: Their meaning is not that there is an essence and it has an attribute and they are actually identical as you imagine. Rather, their meaning is that His essence, exalted, produces what would be produced by both an essence and an attribute together. For example, your essence is not sufficient for the disclosure of things to you; rather, you need the attribute of knowledge that subsists in you. Unlike His essence, which does not need an attribute subsisting in it for the disclosure and manifestation of things; rather, all concepts are disclosed to Him by virtue of His essence. So His essence in this respect is the reality of knowledge. Similarly for power: His essence is effective by itself, not by an additional attribute subsisting in it, as in our essences. So it in this respect is the reality of power. On this basis, the essence and attributes are united in reality but distinct in consideration and concept. Its ultimate conclusion, when examined, is the negation of attributes while their results and fruits are obtained from the essence alone.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage responds to a hypothetical question about how an attribute could be identical with the essence, given our experience that attributes are distinct. The answer clarifies the philosophers' position: they do not claim that there is an essence and a separate attribute that are somehow one. Rather, they mean that God's essence alone suffices to produce the effects that we attribute to attributes. For example, humans need knowledge as a separate quality to know, but God knows everything by His essence alone. Similarly, God's power is His essence being effective. Thus, in reality, the essence and attributes are one; the distinction is only in our conceptual analysis. The ultimate consequence is the denial of attributes while affirming that their functions are performed by the essence itself.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers affirm real, distinct attributes coexisting with the essence, like the Ash'aris do.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the philosophers deny real attributes; the distinction is merely conceptual. The essence alone produces the effects.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, how does God's knowledge differ from human knowledge?
A) God needs an attribute of knowledge.
B) God knows by His essence alone.
C) God's knowledge is created.
D) God's knowledge is limited.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that God's essence alone produces the effects of attributes, so attributes are not real but merely conceptual distinctions.

Philosophers' Argument: Being Agent and Recipient
Philosophers' argument and brief reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that additional attributes would make God both agent and recipient, and the theological response rejecting that analysis.

Core Idea
If God had an attribute, He would be both its creator and its container—this is the crux of the debate.

The philosophers challenge the Ash'ari doctrine of additional attributes by pointing out a seeming contradiction: if God has a distinct attribute, He must be both the agent who creates it and the recipient in whom it subsists. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the terse reply, setting the stage for deeper debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for its rhythm, then read the English translation to grasp the basic claim and response.
Notice the compact style: the argument is given in one sentence, the answer in another.
2
Identify the two positions
The philosophers present a conditional: if additional attribute, then impossible consequence. The author rejects the impossibility. Identify each side's claim.
The author says 'We do not concede its falsehood'—this is a refutation of the premise.
3
Analyze the logic step by step
Use the guided breakdown to follow the argument: why would God be both agent and recipient? What is the supposed impossibility?
The philosophers rely on the idea that one thing cannot be both the source and the recipient of the same property.
4
Reflect on the deeper debate
Consider why this argument matters for divine simplicity. The author says the discussion has already preceded—this is part of a larger contest between philosophers and theologians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الحكماء بأنه لو كان له صفة زائدة على ذاته لكان هو فاعلاً لتلك الصفة وقابلاً لها أيضاً لقيامها بذاته، وقد تقدم بطلانه. والجواب: لا نسلم بطلانه وقد تقدم الكلام عليه.
The philosophers argued that if He had an additional attribute on His essence, then He would be the agent of that attribute and also receptive to it because it subsists in His essence. Its falsehood has already been stated. The answer: We do not concede its falsehood, and the discussion on it has already preceded.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the philosophers against the existence of additional attributes distinct from God's essence. Their reasoning is that if God had an attribute that subsists in Him, He would have to be both the creator of that attribute (as the ultimate agent) and the recipient of it (since it inheres in Him). They consider this combination impossible. The answer provided by the author is succinct: we reject the claim that this is impossible, and we have already refuted it in previous discussions. This sets up a continuation of the debate.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument against additional attributes rests on a claimed impossibility of God being both agent and recipient, but the Ash'ari response denies that such a combination is impossible.

Essence and Attributes: One Reality, Distinct Concepts
Al-Jurjani explains that attributes are not additional entities but the essence itself fulfills their functions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that God's essence and attributes are identical in reality but distinct in concept, and how this avoids anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
God's essence itself produces the effects that in creatures require separate attributes.

This passage from al-Jurjani clarifies a subtle point in Ash'ari theology. Many think that saying 'attributes are one with the essence' is a contradiction or leads to anthropomorphism. Al-Jurjani explains that it means the divine essence suffices for what attributes do in us. Let's read his precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast between God and creatures in the examples.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms essence (ذات), attribute (صفة), reality (الحقيقة), consideration (الاعتبار), and concept (المفهوم). Understand their roles in the argument.
3
Work through the examples
The passage gives two examples: knowledge and power. For each, note how God's essence is sufficient without a separate attribute, unlike in humans.
This illustrates the core idea.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The final sentences state the principle: essence and attributes are united in reality, differing by consideration and concept. Understand what this implies about the attributes' reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مخيل لا يمكن أن يصدق به كما في سائر القضايا المخيلة التي يمتنع التصديق بها فلا حاجة بنا إلى الاستدلال على بطلانه قلت : ليس معنى ما ذكروه أن هناك ذاتا وله صفة وهما متحدان حقيقة كما تخيلته بل معناه أن ذاته تعالى يترتب عليه ما يترتب على ذات وصفة معاً، مثلاً ذاتك ليست كافية في انكشاف الأشياء عليك بل تحتاج في ذلك إلى صفة العلم التي تقوم بك بخلاف ذاته تعالى فإنه لا يحتاج في انكشاف الأشياء وظهورها عليه إلى صفة تقوم به، بل المفهومات بأسرها منكشفة عليه لأجل ذاته تعالى فذاته بهذا الاعتبار حقيقة العلم وكذا الحال في القدرة فإن ذاته تعالى مؤثرة بذاتها لا بصفة زائدة عليها كما في ذواتنا، فهي بهذا الاعتبار حقيقة القدرة وعلى هذا تكون الذات والصفات متحدة في الحقيقة متغايرة بالاعتبار والمفهوم، ومرجعه إذا حقق إلى نفي الصفات مع حصول نتائجها وثمراتها من الذات وحدها.
It is a fantasy that cannot be believed, like other fantastical propositions that cannot be assented to, so we have no need to argue for its falsity. I say: The meaning of what they mentioned is not that there is an essence and it has an attribute and they are actually united, as you imagined. Rather its meaning is that His essence, exalted, has the consequence of what results from both an essence and an attribute together. For example, your essence is not sufficient for the disclosure of things to you, but you need for that the attribute of knowledge that subsists in you, unlike His essence, exalted, for He does not need, for the disclosure of things and their appearance to Him, an attribute subsisting in Him. Rather all concepts are disclosed to Him because of His essence. So His essence in this respect is the reality of knowledge. Similarly, the case is with power, for His essence, exalted, is effective by itself, not by an additional attribute as in our essences. So it in this respect is the reality of power. And on this basis, the essence and the attributes are united in reality, differing by consideration and concept. And its import, when investigated, is the negation of attributes while obtaining their results and fruits from the essence alone.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defines the Ash'ari position on the relation between God's essence and attributes. It begins by dismissing a misconception: that attributes are united with the essence in a literal, anthropomorphic sense. The correct understanding is that God's essence itself fulfills the functions that in created beings require a separate attribute. For example, knowledge in humans requires a faculty, but in God, His essence is such that all concepts are disclosed to Him. Therefore, His essence is, in that respect, the reality of knowledge. Similarly, His essence is effective by itself, so it is the reality of power. Thus, the essence and attributes are one in reality (ḥaqīqa) but multiple in consideration (i'tibār) and concept (mafhūm). The net result, when investigated, is the negation of additional attributes while affirming their outcomes from the essence alone.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The misunderstanding that Ash'aris believe attributes are literally two entities united with the essence, leading to anthropomorphism.
Correction: Al-Jurjani clarifies that attributes are not additional entities; the essence itself produces their effects, so there is only one reality.

Checkpoint

A) They are two distinct entities united in one subject.
B) They are one and the same in reality but distinct in concept.
C) The attributes are separate from the essence.
D) The attributes are not real.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position preserves divine simplicity by identifying attributes with the essence, while still affirming that God's essence does what attributes do in creatures.

Circularity in the Simplicity Argument
Al-Jurjani criticizes a circular argument concerning God's simplicity and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that God's simplicity implies the denial of attributes is logically circular.

Core Idea
Simplicity cannot be used to deny attributes if simplicity itself assumes that denial.

Al-Jurjani here exposes a methodological flaw in a common kalām argument. The claim that God's absolute simplicity entails having no attributes is shown to be circular: the premise of simplicity already depends on the denial of attributes. Let's read his precise critique and see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the key terms: بسيط حقيقي and دور.
2
Identify the two key concepts
The passage contrasts 'true simplicity' (بسيط حقيقي) and 'denial of attributes' (نفي الصفات). Understand each concept in context.
Simplicity here means God has no parts and no composition.
3
Grasp the circularity
Al-Jurjani says that simplicity is built upon the denial of attributes. So using simplicity to prove that denial is a vicious circle (دور). Try to state the circular reasoning in your own words.
This is the core teaching point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وقد تقدم بطلانه حيث بين في مباحث العلة والمعلول أن البسيط الحقيقي يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً وفيه نظر لأن كونه تعالى بسطاً حقيقاً مبني على نفي الصفات فالاحتجاج على هذا النفي بما يبتني على البساطة دور فليتأمل .
His saying: 'And its falsity has already been shown where it was explained in the investigations of cause and effect that the true simple is both recipient and agent, but there is a problem: because His being truly simple is based on the denial of attributes, so using [an argument] that is based on simplicity to prove this denial is a vicious circle. So let it be pondered.'
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage points out a methodological flaw in an argument for denying God's attributes. The argument claimed that if God had additional attributes, He would be both agent and recipient of those attributes, which is impossible for a simple being. But the notion that God is absolutely simple (basīṭ ḥaqīqī) itself presupposes that He has no attributes. Therefore, using that simplicity to prove that He has no attributes is circular reasoning (dawr). The commentator says this needs careful consideration.

Key Terms
بسيط حقيقي
دور
نفي الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simplicity is an independent, self-evident premise that logically entails the denial of attributes.
Correction: In fact, the notion that God is truly simple is itself based on the denial of attributes, so using it as a premise for that denial is circular.

Checkpoint
What is the logical problem identified in this passage?
A) The argument proves too much.
B) The argument is circular.
C) The argument is based on a false premise.
D) The argument is not valid.

Key Takeaway
The argument from simplicity to deny attributes is logically circular because simplicity presupposes the denial of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Where you rely on familiar arguments without checking whether they stand up to scrutiny? Commit today to revisit one aspect of your understanding of God's attributes, not as a formality, but as a person who fears being in error.
?

Were your reasons independent or did they assume what you were trying to prove? Resolve to find a stronger, non-circular foundation for that belief by the end of this week.
