السابقة سالبة لا معدولة، فإن اللاتناهي بمعنى الكثرة الغير المتناهية فلا يتصف أيضاً به إلا الكم بالذات أو بالعرض. قوله : (من الكيف قد مر في مباحث الكيفيات النفسانية أن القابل بثبوت القدرة للواجب لا يجعلها مندرجة في جنس الكيف ولا في الأعراض فكأن المراد بالكيف هاهنا مجرد ما لا يقبل قسمة ولا نسبة لذاته وإن لم يكن من الأعراض وهذا القدر يكفي في المقصود إذ التناهي فرع قبول القسمة . قوله : (هو إثبات اللاتناهي (له لأن التعلق وإن لم يكن كما بالذات إلا أنه معروض للكم المنفصل، أعني العدد لتعدد التعلق بتعدد المتعلقات فيتصف تبعاً لما هو من خواص الكم. قوله : ( وإن كان كل ما تتعلق به بالفعل (متناهياً أي ما تتعلق به بالتعلق التأثيري وأما ما الصفات تتعلق به تعلقاً معنوياً فهو غير متناه، وقد أشرنا إلى التعليقين فيما سبق . قوله : ( مطردة في الصفات كلها ليس المراد باطراد الأحكام الثلاثة التفريعية كلها تساويها فيها من كل وجه كيف والحياة صفة حقيقية عارية عن التعلق فلا يجري فيها التناهي وعدمه باعتباره بل الحكم الأخير من الثلاثة الجارية فيها هو سلب التناهي، وإليه أشار بقوله واعتبر في كل صفة ما يناسبها من الأحكام المتفرعة. قوله : ( فلا حاجة إلى (التكرار وإن وقع تكرار في بعضها فهو توطئة لفائدة أخرى كما قال في بحث الإرادة إرادة الله تعالى لتفريع مذاهب الخصوم .
Third: His power, exalted, is infinite, i.e., not described by finitude, neither in essence nor in relation. As for essence, because finitude is a property of quantity, and there is no quantity there, since power in its essence is of the category of quality, so finitude is negated from it. As for relation, its meaning is that the negation of finitude from it is the affirmation of infinitude for it; and the meaning of its infinitude is that its relation does not stop at a limit such that it cannot be related to what is beyond that limit, even if every object of its actual relation is finite, so its actual relations are always finite, but it is potentially infinite always. And these three derivative rulings apply to all attributes, so we do not repeat them. That is, each of the other attributes is eternal, one, and infinite: the attribute of knowledge is eternal, one, and infinite in essence (meaning negation of finitude), and infinite in relation (meaning affirmation of infinitude in its actual relation); and will is likewise, but its relation is potentially infinite as in power. So analogize and consider for each attribute what is appropriate of the derivative rulings; there is no need for repetition. A note: Power is an attribute additional (to the essence) because of what we have demonstrated regarding the affirmation of the addition of attributes in general, and it may be argued... His statement: (i.e., not described by finitude) indicates that the preceding proposition is a negative one, not a modified one; for infinitude in the sense of infinite multiplicity is also only attributed to quantity essentially or accidentally. His statement: (of the category of quality) – it has passed in the investigations of psychological qualities that what is receptive to the affirmation of power for the Necessary does not make it included in the genus of quality or in accidents; so it is as if the meaning of 'quality' here is merely what does not admit division or relation in itself, even if it is not among accidents, and this suffices for the purpose, since finitude is a branch of admitting division. His statement: (it is the affirmation of infinitude for it) because the relation, even if not a quantity in itself, is a subject for discrete quantity, i.e., number, due to the multiplicity of relations with the multiplicity of relata, so it is characterized by it as a consequence of what is a property of quantity. His statement: (even if every object of its actual relation is finite) means what it relates to with an effective relation; as for what the attributes relate to with a conceptual relation, it is infinite, and we have pointed to the two relations previously. His statement: (apply to all attributes) does not mean that the three derivative rulings all apply equally in every respect; how?? For life is a real attribute devoid of relation, so finitude and its opposite do not apply to it with respect to relation; rather, the last of the three rulings that applies to it is the negation of finitude, and to this he pointed by his saying: 'consider for each attribute what is appropriate of the derivative rulings.' His statement: (there is no need for repetition) – and if repetition occurs in some, it is a prelude to another benefit, as he said in the investigation of divine will: 'God's will' to branch off the doctrines of the opponents.)
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage establishes the third branch: that divine power is infinite, and then generalizes the three rulings (eternity, oneness, infinity) to all attributes. The author makes a distinction between infinity in essence and infinity in relation. In essence, power is not finite because finitude is a property of quantity and power is a quality; thus it cannot be characterized by finitude. In relation, infinity means that power's relation (its objects of power) is not limited; it can relate to anything, even though the actual objects of power are finite. This is described as 'actually finite but potentially infinite'. The passage then states that the same three rulings apply to all attributes, with the nuance that some attributes like life have no relation, so only the negation of finitude applies. The marginal comments clarify: the first comment explains that 'not described by finitude' is a simple negation, not a negation of a privative; the second explains that 'quality' here means what does not admit division; the third explains that relation, though not a quantity, is subject to discrete quantity (number) due to multiple relata; the fourth clarifies that actual relation is effective relation while conceptual relation is infinite; the fifth explains that the three rulings are not applied uniformly: for life, only negation of finitude is relevant; the sixth notes that repetition, if it occurs, serves a purpose. The note (تنبيه) reminds that power is an additional attribute, but the argument for that has been given elsewhere.

تعلقاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine power can actually create an infinite number of things simultaneously.
Correction: The text clarifies that power is potentially infinite, not actually infinite. Actual objects of power are always finite.

Checkpoint
Why is divine power said to be infinite in essence?
A) Because it can create an infinite number of things.
B) Because finitude is a property of quantity, and power is a quality.
C) Because it is an attribute of God.
D) Because it is not limited by time.

Key Takeaway
Divine power is infinite in essence because it is a quality, and infinite in relation because its capacity is limitless, though its actual effects are finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power is infinite in essence because it is a quality, not a quantity, then you cannot measure it or compare it to anything. Where in your life have you been measuring God's ability against your circumstances, treating it as if it were finite? What would it mean for you to stop measuring and start trusting in the qualitative, limitless nature of His power?
?
The text says divine power is potentially infinite in relation, even if actual effects are finite. Think of an area in your life where you have seen only limited results of God's power. Are you willing to believe that His power still holds unlimited potential for that situation, even if you cannot see it yet? What would it look like to pray with that belief today?
?
You learned that finitude belongs to quantity, and power is not quantity. How does this affect the way you think about God's ability to handle the ‘biggest’ problems in your life? Can you recall a moment when you thought a problem was 'too big' for God? How does this passage challenge that thought, and what concrete step will you take to align your thinking with the truth that His power is beyond any measure?

Divine Attributes

The Three Rulings Apply to All Divine Attributes
Generalizes three rulings to all attributes and adds a note on power as additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the three derivative rulings—eternity, oneness, and infinity—apply to all divine attributes, each according to its nature.

Core Idea
Every divine attribute shares the properties of being eternal, one, and infinite, but with nuances for those without relation.

After establishing that power is infinite, the author generalizes the three rulings (eternity, oneness, infinitude) to all attributes. He cautions that these rulings are not mechanical; attention must be paid to the specific nature of each attribute, such as life, which has no relation. This lesson examines this generalization and the concluding note about power being an additional attribute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings
The text mentions that each attribute is eternal (قديمة), one (غير متعددة), and infinite (غير متناهية). These are the three derivative rulings.
The Arabic terms for these are important: قديمة, غير متعددة, غير متناهية.
2
Apply to knowledge and will
The text gives examples: knowledge is eternal, one, infinite in essence (negation of finitude), and infinite in relation (actually finite, potentially infinite). Will is similar.
Notice that 'infinite in relation' for knowledge means it can know all things, but actual knowns are finite?
3
Understand the caution and the note
The text warns that not all attributes have relation (e.g., life). Consider for each what 'infinite' means appropriately. Then the note reminds that power is an additional attribute to the essence.
The marginal comments clarify that life is not described as infinite in relation because it has no relation.

The Third Attribute: Divine Power is Infinite
Explains that divine power is infinite in essence and relation, and the note that it is an additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power is considered infinite both in its essence and in its relation to objects, and how this applies uniformly to all divine attributes.

Core Idea
Divine power's infinity means no limit in essence or potential attachment, not an infinite number of actual objects.

In this passage, the author turns to the third attribute: that divine power is infinite. He carefully distinguishes between infinity in essence and infinity in relation, showing that finitude only applies to quantity, not to quality. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of God's power and its application to all attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the author's own wording set the stage.
Pay attention to the parentheses – they are part of the original text.
2
Distinguish infinity in essence from infinity in relation
Notice that the author first explains why power is not finite in essence (it is a quality, not quantity) and then why it is not finite in relation (its attachment has no limit).
The key point: potential infinity vs actual finitude.
3
Recognize the three derivative rulings
The text mentions three rulings (eternal, not multiple, infinite) that apply to all attributes. See how they are uniform and why repetition is avoided.
This shows the systematic nature of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثالث قدرته تعالى غير متناهية ) أي ليست موصوفة بالتناهي ( لا) ذاتاً و لا تعلقاً، أما ذاتاً فلأن التناهي من خواص الكم ) ولا كم ثمة إذا القدرة بحسب ذاتها من الكيف فيسلب عنها التناهي ( وإما تعلقا فمعناه ) أي معنى سلب التناهي عنه هو إثبات اللاتناهي له ومعنى لا تناهيه ) أن تعلقها لا يقف عند حد لا يمكن تعلقها بغيره )، أي بما وراء ذلك الحد ) وإن كان كل ما تتعلق به بالفعل متناهياً فتعلقهاتها متناهية بالفعل ) دائماً ( غير متناهية بالقوة ) دائماً ( وهذه الأحكام ) الثلاثة التفريعية ( مطردة في الصفات كلها فلا نكررها يعني أن كل واحدة من سائر الصفات قديمة وغير متعددة وغير متناهية فصفة العلم قديمة وواحدة وغير متناهية ذاتا بمعنى التناهي، وغير متناهية تعلقاً بمعنى إثبات اللاتناهي في تعلقها بالفعل والإادة أيضاً كذلك، لكن تعلقها غير متناه بالقوة كما في القدرة وعلى هذا فقس واعتبر في كل صفة ما يناسبها من الأحكام المتفرعة فلا حاجة إلى التكرار ) تنبيه : ( القدرة صفة زائدة ) على الذات ( لما بيناه من إثبات زيادة الصفات على وجه عام
The third: His power, exalted is He, is infinite – that is, it is not described by finitude, neither in essence nor in relation. As for essence, because finitude is a property of quantity, and there is no quantity here, since power in its essence is of the quality kind, so finitude is negated from it. As for relation, its meaning is that its attachment does not stop at a limit beyond which it cannot attach to something else, even if everything it actually attaches to is finite, so its attachments are finitely actual, but infinitely potential always. And these three derivative rulings apply uniformly to all attributes, so we will not repeat them, meaning that each of the other attributes is eternal, not multiple, and infinite: the attribute of knowledge is eternal, one, and infinite in essence (meaning not finite), and infinite in relation (meaning affirming infinity in its actual attachments); likewise will, but its attachment is infinitely potential as in power. So analogize and consider for each attribute what suits it of the derivative rulings – no need for repetition. A note: Power is an additional attribute to the essence, based on what we have established of the addition of attributes in general.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage defines the third attribute of God: that His power is infinite. It begins by stating that power is not described by finitude, neither in its essence nor in its relation to objects. The reason it is not finite in essence is that finitude is a property of quantity, and power belongs to the category of quality, not quantity, so it cannot be finite. Concerning relation, the meaning of non-finiteness is that power can attach to any possible object without limit; there is no object beyond which it cannot attach. Although the actual objects of power are finite at any given time, the potential objects are always infinite. The passage then notes that these three derivative rulings – being eternal, not multiple, and infinite – apply uniformly to all divine attributes, so there is no need to repeat them for each attribute. It gives the example of knowledge and will, showing that they too are infinite in their potential. The final note asserts that power is an additional attribute to the essence, referencing the earlier general proof of attribute addition.

Key Terms
قدرة
تناهي
كم
كيف
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'infinite power' means God actually creates an infinite number of objects at once or that power is an infinite quantity.
Correction: The passage clarifies that infinity applies to potential, not actual, attachments; power is a quality, not a quantity.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is divine power not finite in its essence?
A) Because it is a quality, and finitude is a property of quantity.
B) Because it can create an infinite number of objects.
C) Because it is eternal.
D) Because it is an additional attribute.

Key Takeaway
Divine power is infinite in essence because it is a quality, not a quantity, and infinite in relation because it can potentially attach to any object without limit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's power is infinite in potential, yet you often see only finite outcomes in your life. Do you truly believe that He can act in ways you haven't witnessed? How does this challenge you when you face a situation that feels hopeless?
?
The passage teaches that divine power is a quality, not a quantity. Are you measuring God's involvement in your life by the number of 'blessings' you count, rather than recognizing His power as a constant, qualitative presence? What would change if you shifted your focus?
?
All divine attributes are infinite—knowledge, will, power. If God's will is also infinite in its attachments, does that mean He has a specific plan for you that may be beyond your understanding? How willing are you to submit to a will you cannot fully comprehend?

The First Mu'tazila Argument: Shared Cause
First Mu'tazila argument and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazila argument against divine power creating bodies and the refutation based on the possibility of different causes for the same effect.

Core Idea
Shared effect does not require identical cause; a unique quality may separate divine from created.

The Mu'tazila argue that all observed powers share unfitness to create bodies, so if divine power were similar, it too would be unfit. This lesson presents that argument and the response showing that the shared effect can arise from different causes, and that created power may have a distinct quality causing unfitness.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage for the first argument. Identify the premise: all witnessed powers lack fitness to create bodies, so by analogy, divine power would too.
2
Understand the response
Read the response. Notice it attacks the assumption that a shared effect requires a shared cause. The Mu'tazila themselves allow different causes for the same effect.
Look for the example of ugliness.
3
Analyze the specific counter
The response further suggests that created power may have an extra quality which causes unfitness, absent in divine power. Why is that a valid move?
This refutes the analogy by denying the shared cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تحتج المعتزلة على نفيه بوجهين الأول القدر في الشاهد مشتركة في عدم صلاحيتها لخلق الأجسام والحكم المشترك يجب تعليله بالعلة المشتركة ولا مشترك بينها سوى كونها قدرة فلو كان لله تعالى قدرة لم تصلح لخلق الأجسام لأن علة موجودة فيها أيضاً والجواب أن التعليل بالعلل المختلفة جائز عندكم فإن القبح حكم واحد وقد عللتموه تارةً بكون الشيء ظلماً وأخرى بكونه جهلاً إلى غير ذلك وكذا صحة الرؤية معللة عندكم بخصوصيات المرئيات وهو الحق لجواز اشتراك المختلفات في لازم واحد ثم نقول لم لا يجوز اشتراك القدرة الحادثة في صفة غير موجودة في القدرة القديمة تكون تلك الصفة علة لعدم صلاحيتها فلا يتعدى الحكم إلى القديمة وعدم الوجدان لا يدل على عدم الوجود أي عدم وجداننا لتلك الصفة لا يدل على عدم وجودها في نفسها الثاني القدرة في الشاهد مختلفة اختلافاً ظاهراً ففي الغائب إن كانت القدرة مثلها أي مثل إحدى القدرة التي في الشاهد لم تصلح قدرة الغائب لخلق الأجسام كنظيرتها وإلا لم تكن مخالفتها لها أشد من مخالفة بعضها لبعض فلم تصلح لذلك أيضاً والجواب منع
And the Mu'tazila may argue for its negation with two aspects. First: power in the witnessed (world) shares in its unfitness to create bodies, and a shared judgment must be caused by a shared cause, and there is nothing shared among them except being power; so if God had power, it would not be fit to create bodies, because the same cause exists in it too. The answer: it is permissible for you to attribute [the same effect] to different causes; for ugliness is one judgment and you have sometimes attributed it to a thing being oppression and sometimes to it being ignorance, etc., and similarly the soundness of vision is attributed by you to specificities of visible objects, and this is correct because different things can share a concomitant. Then we say: why is it not permissible for created power to share a quality not present in eternal power, such that that quality is the cause of its unfitness, so the judgment does not transfer to the eternal? And non-finding does not indicate non-existence—that is, our not finding that quality does not indicate its non-existence in itself. Second: power in the witnessed (world) differs manifestly; so in the unseen, if power is similar to it—i.e., similar to one of the powers in the witnessed—then the unseen power would not be fit to create bodies, like its counterpart; but if not, then its difference from it is not more severe than the mutual differences of some [witnessed powers] among themselves, so it would not be fit for that either. The answer: it is denied.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from the Mu'tazila against the view that divine power is capable of creating bodies, along with the responses. The first argument observes that all powers in the witnessed world share the property of being unable to create bodies. Since a shared judgment must have a shared cause, and the only commonality is being power itself, it follows that if God had power, it too would lack the capacity to create bodies. The response counters by pointing out that the Mu'tazila themselves allow different causes to produce the same effect (e.g., ugliness from oppression or ignorance). Similarly, one can posit that created power possesses a specific quality absent in divine power, which prevents it from creating bodies, but this quality does not exist in the divine, so the judgment does not transfer. Moreover, our inability to perceive such a quality does not prove its non-existence. The second argument notes that witnessed powers are diverse; if divine power resembles one of them, it would be unfit to create bodies like that counterpart; if it does not resemble any, its difference is no more significant than the differences among witnessed powers themselves, so it would still be unfit. The response is simply to deny the premise that similarity or difference in the observed implies the same in the unseen, as the argument fails due to the transcendental nature of divine attributes.

Key Terms
قدرة
شاهد
غائب
علة
صلاحية
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Mu'tazila argument is strong because it uses analogy, but the response shows the causal relationship cannot be generalized across different orders of existence.
Correction: The response demonstrates that the shared effect does not guarantee a shared cause, and that created and divine power may differ in a quality affecting capacity.

Checkpoint
What is the core flaw in the first Mu'tazila argument according to the response?
A) The shared judgment does not require a shared cause.
B) The shared cause might be a quality not present in eternal power.
C) The capacity to create bodies is not a judgment.
D) The Mu'tazila do not believe in divine power.

Key Takeaway
The first Mu'tazila argument fails because a shared effect does not necessitate a shared cause; created and divine power may differ in a quality that affects capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you doubted God's ability to act in your life because your own capabilities fell short. How does this lesson confront your tendency to measure the Creator by the standards of creation?
?
The response argues that not finding a quality doesn't mean it doesn't exist. What is one attribute of God that you struggle to accept because you cannot perceive it in your own experience? Will you now trust His word over your perception?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted on logical consistency at the expense of divine transcendence. In what area of your faith do you demand that God fit into your logical categories? Are you willing to let God be God even when it defies your reasoning?

The Second Mu'tazila Argument: Diversity of Powers
Second Mu'tazila argument and its simple denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Mu'tazila argument based on the diversity of witnessed powers and the simple denial of its analogy in the unseen.

Core Idea
Analogy from diverse created powers cannot determine divine power's capacity.

The Mu'tazila's second argument observes that created powers are diverse; if divine power resembles any, it would be as limited; if it differs, its difference is no greater than differences among created powers, so still limited. The response is a direct denial, showing the argument's weakness in applying observed diversity to the transcendent.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic for the second argument. Note the two-case dilemma: similarity or dissimilarity.
2
Understand the response 'denial'
The response is simply 'منع' (it is denied). What does that mean? It rejects the premise that the analogy holds.
The denial is concise but powerful.
3
Reflect on the argument's weakness
The argument assumes that what is observed (diversity and limitation) must also apply to the unseen divine power. The denial shows that this analogy is not valid due to the transcendence of God.

Mu'tazila's First Proof of Incapacity and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents Mu'tazila's first proof of incapacity and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first argument that God's power cannot create bodies, and how al-Jurjani refutes it by breaking the analogy between contingent and divine power.

Core Idea
A shared effect does not require a shared cause; different causes can yield the same result.

This passage presents a key objection from the Mu'tazila against the efficacy of divine power, followed by a precise theological refutation. The Mu'tazila argue that since all observed powers share the common property of being unable to create bodies, and that common property must stem from a common cause—namely being power itself—God's power would also be incapable. Al-Jurjani's response dismantles this by showing that common judgments can arise from different causes, and that contingent power may have an additional quality absent in God's power.

Guided Path
1
Read the Argument and Refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the structure: first the Mu'tazila's proof, then the answer.
The argument is based on analogy from the witnessed realm.
2
Identify the Logical Structure of the Proof
Break down the Mu'tazila's reasoning: (1) All contingent powers are incapable of creating bodies. (2) A common judgment needs a common cause. (3) The only common factor is 'being power'. (4) Therefore, any power, including God's, would be incapable.
This is a strict parallelism argument.
3
Analyze the Refutation
Al-Jurjani gives three counter-points: (a) The same judgment (e.g., ugliness) can have different causes. (b) Contingent power may have a quality not present in eternal power that causes its incapacity. (c) Our failure to find such a quality does not prove it doesn't exist.
The refutation shows the analogy is not necessary.
4
Reflect on the Principle
Consider how this refutation protects divine transcendence. The key is that we cannot transfer a ruling from contingent to divine without proof of sameness in all relevant aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول القدر في الشاهد مشتركة في عدم صلاحيتها لخلق الأجسام والحكم المشترك يجب تعليله بالعلة المشتركة ولا مشترك بينها سوى كونها قدرة فلو كان لله تعالى قدرة لم تصلح لخلق الأجسام لأن علة موجودة فيها أيضاً والجواب أن التعليل بالعلل المختلفة جائز عندكم فإن القبح حكم واحد وقد عللتموه تارةً بكون الشيء ظلماً وأخرى بكونه جهلاً إلى غير ذلك، وكذا صحة الرؤية معللة عندكم بخصوصيات المرئيات وهو الحق لجواز اشتراك المختلفات في لازم واحد ثم نقول لم لا يجوز اشتراك القدرة الحادثة في صفة غير موجودة في القدرة القديمة تكون تلك الصفة علة لعدم صلاحيتها فلا يتعدى الحكم إلى القديمة وعدم الوجدان لا يدل على عدم الوجود أي عدم وجداننا لتلك الصفة لا يدل على عدم وجودها في نفسها
The first: [that] power in the witnessed realm is common in its lack of suitability for creating bodies, and a common judgment must be attributed to a common cause, and there is nothing common among them except being power. So if God were to have power, it would not be suitable for creating bodies, because the cause present in them also [would apply]. The answer: that attribution to different causes is permissible according to you, for ugliness is a single judgment and you have attributed it sometimes to the thing being injustice and sometimes to its being ignorance, etc. Likewise, the validity of vision is attributed by you to specificities of the seen objects, and this is correct because it is permissible for different things to share a single necessary consequence. Then we say: why is it not permissible for contingent power to share a quality not present in eternal power, such that that quality would be the cause of its lack of suitability, so the judgment does not extend to the eternal? And not finding [that quality] does not indicate its non-existence—meaning, our not finding that quality does not indicate its non-existence in itself.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the Mu'tazila against God's power being capable of creating bodies, followed by its refutation. The Mu'tazila argue that all observed powers share the common property of being incapable of creating bodies; since the common judgment of incapacity must be caused by a common attribute, and the only common attribute is power itself, any power (including God's) would be incapable. The answer refutes the necessity of a single cause for a common judgment, citing examples like ugliness which they attribute to different causes. Further, contingent power may possess an additional quality absent in eternal power, breaking the analogy. Finally, our inability to perceive such a quality does not prove its absence.

Key Terms
قدرة
صلاحية
علة
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila's argument is based on an analogy of equality; the answer shows that strict parallelism fails because different causes can produce the same effect.
Correction: The Mu'tazila's argument is not an analogy but a logical deduction from a principle of causality. The refutation does not deny the principle but shows that the shared judgment does not necessitate a shared cause, and that additional factors may be present.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does the fact that contingent power is unsuitable for creating bodies not imply that Divine power is also unsuitable?
A) Because contingent power is not a real power.
B) Because different causes can produce the same effect, and contingent power may have an additional quality.
C) Because God's power is different in essence from all powers.
D) Because the Mu'tazila are mistaken in their premise.

Key Takeaway
A common effect does not prove a common cause; the analogy from contingent to divine power is broken by the possibility of distinct causes and qualities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume that because something is true in your limited experience, it must also be true of Allah, what part of your worship or belief reflects that assumption? Will you actively challenge that assumption today by consciously reflecting on the absolute difference between the Creator and creation?

The Mu'tazila failed to find a quality in Allah's power because they only looked at created powers. Refuting the Objection Against Divine Power
Al-Jurjani refutes an objection against divine power by applying the same logic to the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani refutes the Mu'tazila's objection about God's power by turning their logic against the divine essence itself.

Core Idea
Turning an opponent's logic on its own foundation is a powerful kalam strategy.

Al-Jurjani here engages in a sophisticated dialectical move. The Mu'tazila argue that God cannot have power because power would be like human power. Al-Jurjani retorts that the same reasoning would apply to God's essence, which they accept. This forces them into a contradiction or forces them to revise their argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
Read the passage and identify the Mu'tazila's implied claim that God would not be powerful based on their argument.
The objection is introduced by the phrase 'وقد تحتج المعتزلة'.
2
See the Counterattack via Essence
Notice how al-Jurjani applies the same logic to the essence itself, arguing that if being an essence makes one incapable of creating bodies, then God's essence would also be incapable, which is absurd.
The key phrase is 'دليل المعتزلة ينتقض بالذات'.
3
Consider the Limits of the Refutation
Why is the refutation not applied to hearing and sight? Because they are not sources of effect (مبدأ التأثير), unlike essence and power.
Reading aloud clarifies why hearing and sight are excluded.
4
Reflect on the Open Conclusion
The passage ends with 'فتأمل' (so reflect), indicating that the issue is not fully settled and requires further thought.
This is a hallmark of scholarly honesty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وقد تحتج المعتزلة) نقل عنه رحمه الله تعالى أنه يلزم من هذا أن لا يكون الباري تعالى قادراً أيضاً، وهم لا يقولون به وقال الأستاذ المحقق : دليل المعتزلة ينتقض بالذات بأن يقال الذوات في الشاهد مشتركة في عدم صلاحيتها بخلق الأجسام إلى آخر الدليل فلو تم هذا الدليل في القدرة لزم عدم تحقق ذات الواجب، وإنما لم يرد النقض بالسمع والبصر ونحوهما لأن شيئاً منها على تقدير تسليم المعتزلة ثبوته ليس منشأ للتأثير فلا يتأتى أن يقال : فلو كان لله تعالى سمع لم يصلح بخلق الأجسام لعدم بطلان التالي بخلاف نفس الذات فإنه مبدأ للتأثير وبخلاف القدرة فإنها معنى من شأنها تأتي الإيجاد بها، وقد يجاب بأن اللازم هو ثبوت ذات مشتركة غير صالحة بخلق الأجسام، والحال كذلك عند من يقول بمماثلة ذاته سائر الذوات وليس بشيء، بل اللازم حينئذ لا تصلح ذاته المخصوصة لذلك لأن خصوصيات ذوات الممكنات إنما لم تصلح لذلك لكونها ذواتاً وهذه العلة موجودة في خصوصية ذاته تعالى فليتأمل
His saying: 'And the Mu'tazila may argue' — it is reported from him (may God have mercy on him) that from this it follows that God the Exalted would also not be powerful, and they do not say this. The professor verified said: The proof of the Mu'tazila is refuted by the essence itself, by saying: essences in the witnessed realm share in their lack of suitability for creating bodies, to the end of the proof. If this proof were valid for power, the non-existence of the Necessary Being would follow. However, the refutation is not applied to hearing, sight, and the like, because none of them—on the assumption of the Mu'tazila's concession of their existence—is a source of effect. So it cannot be said: 'If God had hearing, it would not be suitable for creating bodies' because the consequent is not false, unlike the essence itself, for it is the source of effect, and unlike power, for it is a meaning whose function is to bring about existence. And it may be answered that the necessary conclusion is the existence of a common essence that is not suitable for creating bodies, and such is the case for those who say His essence is similar to all essences, but that is nothing. Rather, the necessary conclusion then is that His specific essence is not suitable for that, because the particularities of possible essences are not suitable for that because they are essences, and this cause is present in the particularity of His essence. So reflect.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: the Mu'tazila's argument would imply that God is not powerful, which they deny. The reply (from 'the verifying professor') is that the same logic can be turned against the essence itself: if being an essence makes one incapable of creating bodies, then God's essence would also be incapable, which is absurd. The objection is not applied to hearing or sight because they are not sources of effect. However, a potential answer is that the necessary conclusion is a common essence, not the specific essence of God. But the commentary dismisses this, noting that the cause—being an essence—is present in God's particular essence, so the difficulty remains.

Key Terms
ذات
مبدأ التأثير
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage provides a definitive refutation that completely settles the issue.
Correction: The passage ends with 'فتأمل' (so reflect), indicating that the matter is not fully resolved and requires further contemplation.

Checkpoint
Why does the objection not apply the same reasoning to God's hearing or sight?
A) Because the Mu'tazila do not accept that God has hearing or sight.
B) Because hearing and sight are not sources of effect (منشأ للتأثير).
C) Because hearing and sight are attributes of perfection.
D) Because the proof only works for power.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani shows that the Mu'tazila's argument against God's power would also undermine the essence itself, exposing a deeper inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend your beliefs about God, are you willing to apply the same standard of proof to your own position as you demand of others? How does al-Jurjani's method challenge you to be more consistent in your own reasoning?
?
The passage ends with 'so reflect.' Have you ever considered that some of your own theological assumptions might be self-defeating if followed to their logical conclusion? Will you examine your own beliefs with the same rigor tomorrow?
?
If you were in a debate with someone who held a flawed argument, would you have the courage to turn their logic back on itself, even if it forced you to refine your own understanding? How can you practice this intellectual humility in your study of aqeedah this week?

Mu'tazila's Second Proof of Incapacity and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents the second Mu'tazilite proof from variation of capacities and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument from variation of capacities and how al-Jurjani refutes it by rejecting the analogy and appealing to particularities (خصوصية).

Core Idea
Similarity in kind does not force identical rulings; specific differences matter.

Al-Jurjani continues his refutation of the Mu'tazila's proofs for the impossibility of the attribute of power (قدرة) in God. Here he presents their second proof, which argues from the manifest variation among created capacities. He then shows why the argument fails by clarifying what 'likeness' means and by appealing to the role of particularities (خصوصية) in grounding different judgments. This lesson will walk you through the proof and its refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Understand the Mu'tazilite argument from observed variation: created powers differ manifestly, so if God's power resembles one of them, it shares its limitation; if not, its difference is no greater than differences among created powers, so it still cannot be suitable for creating bodies.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if like... then...; if not like... then...'.
2
Identify the key analogy
The Mu'tazila assume that similarity in kind (مماثلة) implies functional equivalence. They argue that either God's power is like some created power (and thus limited) or it is unlike, but then its unlikeness is not more extreme than differences among created powers, so it remains unsuitable.
3
Examine the refutation
Al-Jurjani simply rejects (منع) the argument. He then explains why the analogy fails: generic likeness does not entail identical judgments because individuals of the same kind have particularities (خصوصية) that can ground distinct rulings.
The refutation has two layers: outright rejection and a nuanced explanation.
4
Grasp the concept of particularity
Individuals sharing the same reality (e.g., created powers) are distinguished by particularities. Those particularities can be the basis for specific judgments, so generic resemblance alone does not determine capability.
This is a key metaphysical principle in Islamic theology.
5
See the conclusion
The proof fails; God's power is not shown to be unsuitable for creating bodies. The Mu'tazilite argument is invalid because it ignores the role of particularities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني القدرة في الشاهد مختلفة اختلافاً ظاهراً ففي الغائب إن كانت القدرة مثلها أي مثل إحدى القدرة التي في الشاهد لم تصلح قدرة الغائب لخلق الأجسام كنظيرتها وإلا لم تكن مخالفتها لها أشد من مخالفة بعضها لبعض فلم تصلح لذلك أيضاً والجواب منع قوله : ففي الغائب إن كانت القدرة مثلها إلخ ) الظاهر أن المراد بالمماثلة هو النوعي فيرد عليه أن الأفراد المتفقة الحقيقة لا بد أن تتمايز بخصوصية قطعاً فيجوز أن تكون تلك الخصوصية منشأ لحكم مخصوص وقد تحمل المثلية على الاتحاد في جميع الاتحاد في الحقيقة ولا يخفى بعده. أن مخالفتها للقدرة الحادثة ليست أشد من مخالفة بعضها لبعض ) فلا يلزم عدم صلاحيتها لما ذكر
The second: power in the witnessed realm differs manifestly. In the unseen, if power is like it—i.e., like one of the powers in the witnessed realm—then the unseen power would not be suitable for creating bodies like its counterpart; otherwise, its difference from it would not be more severe than the difference between some [powers] and others, so it would not be suitable for that either. The answer: rejection. His saying: 'For in the unseen, if power is like it, etc.'—apparently what is meant by likeness is generic. So it is objected that individuals sharing the same reality must necessarily be distinguished by a particularity, and that particularity may be the source of a specific judgment. Likeness may also be interpreted as unity in all respects, but its remoteness is not hidden. That its difference from contingent power is not more severe than the difference between some [contingent powers] and others, so its lack of suitability for what was mentioned does not follow.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite proof and its refutation. The proof runs: observed powers vary greatly; if God's power resembles one of them, it will share its incapacity; if it does not, its difference is not greater than differences among created powers, so it still cannot be suitable. The answer is simply to reject (منع) the argument. The commentary clarifies that 'likeness' likely means generic similarity, but individuals of the same genus are distinguished by particularities that can ground different rulings. Moreover, complete unity of power is far-fetched. Hence the conclusion that God's power is unsuitable does not follow.

Key Terms
مماثلة
مخالفة
خصوصية
نوعي

Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that generic resemblance does not entail identical rulings because individuals have particularities (خصوصية) that can be the basis for distinct judgments.

Checkpoint
What is the core of the response to the second proof?
A) It is impossible for God's power to resemble created powers.
B) The rejection of the argument (منع) and the explanation that specific differences can vary rulings.
C) Created powers are actually all alike in capability.
D) God's power is infinitely greater than any created power.

Key Takeaway
The second proof fails because generic resemblance does not force identical rulings; particularities (خصوصية) can break the analogy.

Proof That Divine Power Encompasses All Possible Things
Proof that Divine power covers all possible things based on equality of essence's relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the proof that Divine power extends to all possible entities, including its dependence on the Ash'ari position on non-existents.

Core Idea
Divine power reaches all possibles because the essence relates equally to them, given that non-existents have no distinctions.

This lesson unpacks a key argument for the comprehensiveness of Divine power. The proof rests on two foundational points: what determines power (the essence) and what qualifies something to be an object of power (possibility). From there, it contends that since the essence relates equally to all possible things, power must extend to them all. However, this conclusion hinges on a specific metaphysical assumption about the nature of non-existent entities—a point that distinguishes the Ash'ari position from that of the Mu'tazila and philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and its components
Read the passage and locate the main claim (Divine power encompasses all possibles). Then find the two components of the proof: the determinant (مقتضى) and the condition (مصحح). Ask yourself why each is necessary.
The determinant is the essence, the condition is possibility.
2
Trace the equality of relation
Notice that the essence's relation to all possible things is equal. Why does this follow from the nature of non-existents according to Ahl al-Haqq? Consider the view that non-existents are pure negations with no distinctions.
If non-existents were distinct, the relation might not be equal.
3
Understand the opposition's views
The passage mentions two alternative positions: the Mu'tazila believe non-existents are distinct things, and the philosophers believe matter has dispositions. How would these views challenge the equality of relation?
Both alternatives would allow for inequality, hence the proof depends on rejecting them.
4
Synthesize the full argument
Combine all the pieces: essence → power, possibility → object of power, equal relation → if power to some then to all. The argument only works if non-existents are not distinct (Ash'ari view). Reflect on why this metaphysical assumption is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني : ( فيأن قدرته تعالى تعم سائر الممكنات أي جميعها ( والدليل عليه أن المقتضى للقدرة ) هو ( الذات ( لوجوب استناد صفاته إلى ذاته والمصحح للمقدورية) هو (الإمكان لأن الوجوب والامتناع الذاتيين يحيلان المقدورية ( ونسبة الذات إلى جميع الممكنات على السوية وإذا أثبتت قدرته على بعضها تثبت على كلها ( وهذا ) الاستدلال ) بناء على ما ذهب إليه أهل الحق من أن المعدوم ليس بشيء وإنما هو نفي محض ولا امتياز فيه أصلاً ) ولا تخصيص ) قطعاً فلا يتصور اختلاف في نسبة الذات إلى المعدومات بوجه من الوجوه ( خلافاً للمعتزلة و) من أن المعدوم ( لا) مادة له ولا صورة خلافاً للحكماء وإلا لم يمتنع اختصاص البعض بمقدوريته تعالى ( دون بعض كما يقوله الخصم ، فعلى قاعدة الاعتزال جاز أن تكون خصوصية بعض المعدومات الثابتة المتميزة مانعة من تعلق القدرة به وعلى قانون الحكمة جاز أن تستعد المادة لحدوث ممكن دون آخر وعلى التقديرين لا تكون نسبة الذات إلى جميع الممكنات على سواء
The second research: that His power encompasses all possible things, i.e., all of them. The proof for it is that the determinant of power is the essence, because His attributes must be dependent on His essence, and that which renders something an object of power is possibility, because essential necessity and impossibility preclude being an object of power. And the relation of the essence to all possible things is equal. If His power is proven over some, it is proven over all. This proof is based on the position of Ahl al-Haqq that the non-existent is not a thing but a pure negation, having no distinction at all, and absolutely no specification, so no difference can be imagined in the relation of the essence to non-existents in any way—contrary to the Mu'tazila and to those who say the non-existent has neither matter nor form, contrary to the philosophers. Otherwise, it would not be impossible for some to be specifically within His power and others not, as the opponent claims. For on the Mu'tazilite principle, the specific nature of some non-existents that are established and distinct could prevent the attachment of power to it, and on the principle of philosophy, matter is disposed for the occurrence of one possible and not another. On both suppositions, the relation of the essence to all possible things is not equal.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents a new research topic: the comprehensiveness of Divine power over all possible things. The proof has two components: (1) the determinant (مقتضى) of power is the essence, and the condition (مصحح) for being an object of power is possibility (إمكان). (2) The essence relates equally to all possible things because non-existents have no distinctions (according to Ahl al-Haqq). Therefore, if power is established over some, it is over all. The proof relies on the Ash'ari denial of distinct realities for non-existents. The commentary contrasts this with the Mu'tazilite position (non-existents are distinct things) and the philosophers' view (matter has dispositions), both of which would allow unequal relations.

Key Terms
مقتضى
مصحح
إمكان
نسبة الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof works regardless of the ontological status of non-existents.
Correction: The proof explicitly depends on the Ash'ari position that non-existents are pure negations with no distinctions. If non-existents had distinct realities (Mu'tazila) or matter had dispositions (philosophers), the equality of relation would not hold, and the proof would fail.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what condition must be met for something to be an object of Divine power?
A) It must be a created thing.
B) It must be possible (ممكن).
C) It must have an essence.
D) It must be known to God.

Key Takeaway
The comprehensiveness of Divine power is proven by the equality of the essence's relation to all possible things, which itself depends on the Ash'ari doctrine that non-existents have no distinct reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof depends on the equality of God's relation to all possibles—His power is not blocked by any 'worthiness' of the thing. Are there people you have deemed beyond God's forgiveness or guidance? How does this lesson challenge you to view them differently?
?
If God's power encompasses all possible things, then your deepest weakness, your most persistent sin, and your past failure are all within His power to transform. Do you live as if that is true? What one area of your life will you entrust to His power today, not as a thought but as a concrete act of reliance?

Objection: Bodies Must Be Homogeneous for Universal Power
Al-Jurjani discusses the condition of homogeneity and answers the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that bodies must be homogeneous for God's power to be universal, and how al-Jurjani answers it by appealing to the free agent's control over particularities.

Core Idea
Even if bodies differ, God's power is unlimited because He controls all particularities.

Al-Jurjani now addresses a subtle objection: if bodies are not homogeneous in reality, their specific differences might be intrinsic, limiting God's power to create others like them. He explains that such particularities are under God's control, so homogeneity is not necessary for universal power. This exchange sharpens the understanding of divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the condition of homogeneity and why the objector claims it is necessary.
The objector builds on the idea that bodies are composed of identical atoms; if they differ, specification might be due to essence, not will.
2
Understand the objection's logic
The objector argues: if bodies differ in reality, their particularities might be intrinsic, meaning God cannot change them, limiting His power to create other bodies. This challenges universality.
3
Follow the response and the question-answer exchange
Al-Jurjani grants homogeneity for argument's sake but raises a further question: why can't particularity itself be an obstacle? He answers that the free agent can remove any particularity and create another, unless it is a determinant of individualization (which is left open).
The text ends abruptly, but the core answer is given: the free agent has power over particularities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : ولا بد أيضاً من تجانس الأجسام لتركبها من الجواهر الفردة المتماثلة الحقيقة ليكون اختصاص بعضها ببعض الأعراض الإرادة الفاعل المختار إذ مع تخالفها جاز أن يكون ذلك الاختصاص لذواتها فلا قدرة على إيجاد بعض آخر منها ، ( واعلم أن المخالفي نفي هذا الأصل) قوله : (قيل ولا بد أيضاً من تجانس الأجسام إلخ) فإن قلت لو سلم التجانس فلم لا يجوز أن يكون خصوصية بعض الأجسام مانعة مثل خصوصية بعض المعدومات على قاعدة الاعتزال، قلت : تلك الخصوصية لا تكون مستندة حينئذ لا إلى القادر المختار فله أن يعدمها ويوجد خصوصية أخرى لا تمانع القدرة اللهم إلا أن يقال الخصوصية إذا كانت من المشخصات
It was said: There must also be homogeneity of bodies, because they are composed of individual atoms identical in reality, so that the specification of some with some accidents is by the will of the free agent; for if they differ, it is possible that specification is due to their essences, and then there is no power to bring into existence some others. (And know that the objector denies this principle.) His saying: 'It was said there must also be homogeneity of bodies, etc.'—for if you grant homogeneity, why is it not allowed that the particularity of some bodies is an obstacle, like the particularity of some non-existents on the Mu'tazilite principle? I say: that particularity then would not be traceable to the free agent, so He can annihilate it and bring into existence another particularity that does not impede power—unless it is said that the particularity is among the determinants of individualization.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response concerning the need for homogeneity of bodies for the proof of universal power. The objector argues that if bodies are composed of identical atoms, then their differing accidental properties must be due to the free agent's will; if bodies differ in essence, then the specific differences might be intrinsic, limiting God's power to create other bodies. The answer: even if bodies were homogeneous, one could still ask why specificities could not be obstacles. The response is that such specificities are not independent of the free agent; He can remove them and create new ones, except perhaps if the specificities are essential determinants of individualization (which would require further analysis).

Key Terms
تجانس
جواهر فردة
إرادة
فاعل مختار
مشخصات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection shows that God's power is limited unless bodies are homogeneous.
Correction: Al-Jurjani argues that even without homogeneity, God can control particularities, so homogeneity is not necessary for universal power.

Checkpoint
What is the main response to the objection about the particularity of bodies being an obstacle?
A) Such particularities do not exist.
B) The free agent can remove the particularity and create another that does not impede power.
C) Homogeneity prevents any particularity from being an obstacle.
D) The particularity is always due to the agent's will.

Key Takeaway
The free agent's power over particularities ensures universal power even if bodies differ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The objector feared that without homogeneity, God's power would be limited. In your own faith, do you ever limit God by assuming He cannot change certain deep-seated patterns in your life? What one step can you take this week to trust Him with that particularity?

Refuting the Philosophers’ Principle of Divine Unity
Refutes the philosophers' claim that only one effect can proceed from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that only one effect proceeds from God and how it is refuted, including the clarification about divine power.

Core Idea
The claim 'the one does not issue from the one except one' is denied and previously falsified.

This passage confronts a key metaphysical principle advanced by the divine philosophers: from a truly simple being, only one immediate effect can proceed. The author denies this outright, asserting that the philosophers' evidence has already been refuted. Additionally, the passage clarifies that when discussing power, both philosophers and Mu'tazila affirm God's capability, though in different senses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim and refutation
Notice how the philosophers assert that only one effect can proceed from God, and the author simply denies their principle, stating it has been falsified earlier.
3
Understand the nuance about power
The objection about the philosophers and Mu'tazila denying power is answered by distinguishing between power as capability (al-qadiriyya) and its comprehensiveness.
This clarifies that the disagreement is about the scope of power, not its existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأولى (الفلاسفة الإلهيون فإنهم ( قالوا : إنه ( تعالى ( واحد حقيقي فلا : يصدر عنه أثران والصادر عنه ( ابتداء هو ( العقل الأول والبواقي صادرة عنه بالوسائط كما شرحناه ) من قبل والجواب منعه قولهم : الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد ) وما . تمسكوا به في إثباته فقد زيفناه قوله : (الأولى الفلاسفة إلخ ) فإن قيل : الفلاسفة والمعتزلة لا يقولون بالقدرة فلا معنى لعدهم من المخالفين في شمولها قلنا : المراد بالقدرة القادرية أي كونه تعالى قادراً ولا خلاف للمعتزلة في ذلك وكذا للفلاسفة لكن بمعنى لا ينافي الإيجاب كذا في شرح المقاصد والأقرب في الجواب أن نفي عموم القدرة قد يكون بنفي أصلها وقد يكون بنفي وصفها كما في سييجي [؟] مثله
The first (the divine philosophers, for they said: He (Exalted) is truly one, so two effects cannot proceed from Him, and what proceeds from Him initially is the first intellect, and the rest proceed from Him through intermediaries as we have explained before. The answer is to deny their statement: 'The one does not issue from the one except one,' and what they clung to in proving it we have already falsified. His statement: 'The first, the philosophers, etc.' For if it is said: The philosophers and the Mu'tazila do not affirm power, so there is no meaning in counting them among the opponents in its comprehensiveness, we say: By power is meant capability (al-qadiriyya), i.e., that He is capable, and there is no disagreement from the Mu'tazila in that, and likewise from the philosophers, but in a sense that does not negate necessitation (al-ijab). Thus in Sharh al-Maqasid. And the closest in answer is that the negation of the comprehensiveness of power may be by negating its essence or by negating its attribute, as will come later like it.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines and refutes the position of the divine philosophers who claim that from God, who is truly one, only one immediate effect can proceed—the first intellect—and all other beings come into existence through intermediaries. The author gives the answer: he simply denies their principle 'the one does not issue from the one except one,' stating that their evidence for this principle has already been refuted earlier. The passage then includes an explanatory note: an objection is raised that philosophers and Mu'tazila do not even affirm God's power, so why count them as opponents regarding the comprehensiveness of that power? The reply clarifies that 'power' here means capability (al-qadiriyya)—the attribute of being capable—and both groups do affirm that; the philosophers affirm it in a sense compatible with necessitation (al-ijab). The author mentions that the negation of the comprehensiveness of power can be either by denying its essence or its attribute, a topic to be discussed later.

Key Terms

الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد
العقل الأول
القدرة القادرية
الإيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers and Mu'tazila completely deny God's power.
Correction: They deny the comprehensiveness of power, not the attribute itself. Both affirm that God is capable (qadir), but philosophers interpret it in a way compatible with necessitation.

Checkpoint

B) The one does not issue from the one except one
C) The first intellect is eternal
D) The universe is created in time

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' principle of unity is denied outright, and the discussion of power clarifies that the disagreement is about the scope of power, not the existence of the attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, like the philosophers, subconsciously limited Allah's power by assuming He must act in a certain logical way? What specific belief in your life might actually be a hidden restriction on His capability? Commit today to replacing that assumption with the Quranic teaching that He does whatever He wills.
?
The lesson shows that a seemingly rational principle can be false. Is there any theological assumption you hold that you have not examined against revelation? What would it cost you to let it go if it contradicts the Quran and Sunnah? Be honest with yourself and take a step to investigate.
?
The disagreement is about the scope of Allah's power, not whether He has power. Are you truly comfortable with the idea that Allah's power is unlimited and incomprehensible, even when it challenges your understanding of cause and effect? Refutation of the Dualists on God's Power over Evil
Al-Iji refutes the dualists' claim and explains why 'evil' is not attributed to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualists' objection that God cannot have power over evil because it would make Him both good and evil, and the response that God creates all things but is not called 'evil' due to linguistic connotation and lack of scriptural authorization.

Core Idea
God creates evil but is not evil; names depend on revelation, not action.

The dualists, including the Magians, argued that if God had power over evil, He would be both good and evil, so they posited two separate gods. Al-Iji responds by conceding that God creates all goods and evils, but then explains why the term 'evil' (sharr) is not applied to Him: first, because calling Him 'evil' would imply that evil predominates in His actions, and second, because divine names are based on scriptural authorization (tawqif).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Pay attention to the dualists' claim and the structure of the response.
Notice that the response begins with 'We accept the consequent'.
2
Identify the dualist claim
The dualists claim that God cannot have power over evil because if He did, He would be both good and evil. This leads them to affirm two gods.
This claim is the foundation of their dualism.
3
Examine the two reasons
The response gives two reasons why the term 'evil' is not applied to God despite His creating evil. Understand each reason: (1) it would imply evil predominates, (2) lack of scriptural authorization.
The first reason is about linguistic connotation; the second is about divine naming.
4
Reflect on the principle of tawqif
Consider the principle that divine names are based on revelation (tawqif). This is a key theological rule in Islam.
This principle limits how we describe God to only what is revealed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة ( الثالثة : الثنوية ومنهم المجوس ) فإنهم ( قالوا : إنه تعالى لا يقدر على الشر وإلا لكان خيراً شريراً معاً ) فلذلك أثبتوا إلهين كما مر تفصيله والجواب: أنا نلتزم التالي فإنه تعالى خالق للخيرات والشرور كلها ( وإنما لا يطلق لفظ الشرير عليه كما لا يطلق عليه لفظ خالق القردة والخنازيز) مع خالقاً لهما ( لأحد الأمرين، إما لأنه يوهم أن يكون الشر غالباً في فعله كما يقال : فلان شرير أي ذلك مقتضى نحيزته ( أي طبيعته والغالب على هجيراه) أي دأبه وعادته، ( وإما لعدم التوقيف ) من الشرع ( وأسماء الله تعالى توقيفية ) .
The third sect: the dualists, including the Magians, for they said: He, exalted, is not capable of evil, otherwise He would be both good and evil together. Therefore they affirmed two gods as was detailed earlier. The answer: We accept the consequent, for He, exalted, is the creator of all goods and evils. But the word 'evil' (al-sharr) is not applied to Him, just as the word 'creator of apes and pigs' is not applied to Him, though He is their creator, for one of two reasons: either because it would suggest that evil predominates in His action, as one says 'so-and-so is evil' meaning that is the demand of his nature (nahizatihi) and predominant in his habit (hijirah), i.e., his custom and practice; or due to lack of authorization (tawqif) from the Law, and the names of God are based on authorization (tawqifiyya).
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents the claim of the dualists (الثنوية), including the Magians, and the response to it. The passage begins by stating their argument: God cannot have power over evil because if He did, He would be both good and evil simultaneously. Hence they posited two gods, one good and one evil. The response concedes the consequent (التالي) that God is the creator of all things, both good and evil, but then explains why the term 'evil' (شرير) is not applied to Him. Two reasons are given: first, calling Him 'evil' would imply that evil predominates in His actions, which is not the case; second, divine names are based on scriptural authorization (توقيف), and the Law does not authorize that name. The explanation clarifies that God's creating evil does not make Him evil, just as creating apes and pigs does not make Him an ape or pig.

Key Terms
الفرقة الثالثة
الثنوية
المجوس
قدرة
شر
خير
شرير
توقيف
أسماء الله توقيفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates evil, then He must be evil.
Correction: Creating evil does not make one evil; the name 'evil' implies predominance of evil in one's nature or actions, which does not apply to God, and divine names are based on revelation.

Checkpoint
Why do the dualists claim that God cannot have power over evil?
A) Because evil does not exist.
B) Because otherwise He would be both good and evil.
C) Because He is only good.
D) Because evil is not created by God.

Key Takeaway
God's creation of evil does not make Him evil; divine names are determined by revelation, not by our reasoning about His actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness evil in the world, do you find yourself questioning God's goodness or struggling to accept that He created it? How does this lesson's distinction between creation and naming challenge your instinctive reaction?
?
This lesson teaches that divine names are based solely on revelation, not our human sense of justice. Is there a divine name or attribute that you find difficult to embrace because of your own experiences? Will you submit to revelation even when your heart hesitates?
?
Al-Iji says we do not call God 'evil' because it would imply evil predominates in His actions. Do you secretly harbor the belief that evil is more prevalent than mercy in your own life? How can you realign your perception with the Qur'anic emphasis on Allah's mercy over His wrath?

Refuting Al-Nazzam: Relative Ugliness and Capability
Al-Iji presents al-Nazzam's claim and the initial refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's argument that God cannot perform ugly acts and the refutation based on the relativity of ugliness and the distinction between capability and actual doing.

Core Idea
Ugliness is relative to God; capability remains even if the act is not done.

Al-Nazzam claimed that God cannot do ugly acts because doing so would be foolish. Al-Iji responds by pointing out that ugliness is relative to God as owner of all, and even if it were considered ugly, the deterrent (صارف) only prevents occurrence, not the power. This lesson walks through that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Nazzam's argument
Read the passage where al-Nazzam states that God cannot do ugly acts because it would entail foolishness or ignorance.
Notice the reasoning: knowledge of ugliness plus action equals foolishness.
2
Understand the refutation: relativity
Al-Iji's first response is that ugliness is relative; everything belongs to God, so He can do as He wills.
3
Grasp the deterrent argument
Even if ugliness relative to God is granted, it only deters the act, not the capability.
Capability is distinct from actual performance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة ( الرابعة : النظام ومتبعوه قالوا : لا يقدر على ( الفعل ( القبيح لأنه مع العلم بقبحه سفه ودونه جهل وكلاهما نقص) يجب تنزيهه تعالى عنه ( والجواب : أنه لا قبيح بالنسبة إليه فإن الكل ملكه فله أن يتصرف فيه على أي وجه أراد ) وإن سلم ) قبح الفعل بالقياس إليه ( فغايته عدم الفعل لوجود الصارف ) عنه وهو القبح ) وذلك لا ينفي القدرة عليه. قوله : ( وإلا لكان خيراً وشريراً (معاً لا يخفى أن دليلهم على تقدير تمامه لا يثبت مدعاهم لأنه إنما ينفي فعل الشر والمدعى نفي القدرة عليه ومن البين أن القادر على الشر الغير الفاعل إياه لا يكون شريرا . قوله : ( والجواب إنا نلتزم (التالي) هذا على تقدير أن يراد بالخير خالق الخير، وبالشرير خالق الشر، وأما إن أريد بالخير من يغلب خيره على شره وبالشر من يغلب شره على خيره فالجواب منع الملازمة ولما سبق فيه التفصيل اكتفى هاهنا بما ذكره . قوله : (مقتضى نحيزته والغالب على هجيراه النحيزة بالنون المفتوحة والحاء المهملة والزاي المعجمة على وزن فعيلة والهجير بكسر الهاء والجيم المشددة وفتح الراء المهملة وفيه لغات أخرى وهي الهجير على وزن الفسيق والإهجيري بكسر الهمزة وفتح الراء وهجرياً بكسر الهمزة والراء وتشديد الياء . قوله : ( لأنه مع العلم بقبحه سفه قيل : إنما يلزم السفه على تقدير تسليم قبح الفعل بالنسبة إليه تعالى إذا لم تكن جهة الحسن راجحة كثيرة إذ الحكمة حينئذ تقتضي وجود ذلك الفعل ليحصل حسنه الكثير وإن لزم القبح القليل وهذا كما قال الحكماء : الشر داخل في القضاء على سبيل التبع. وهذا إنما يرد عليهم إذا لم يكن كلامهم في القبح المطلق أو الذي جهة قبحه كثيرة راجحة . قوله : ( وذلك لا ينفي القدرة عليه فإن قلت : بل ينفيها إذ المقدور عندنا ما يصح فعله و تركه فلو كان القبيح مقدورا على تقدير تسليم القبح بالقياس إليه تعالى لزم جواز النقص، تعالى ذلك علواً كبيراً قلت : المحذور هاهنا إنما لزم من الامتناع الغيري أعني من وصف القبح الخارج عن ذات الفعل لا منه نفسه فلا ينافي الإمكان الذاتي الكافي في المقدورية.
The fourth sect: al-Nazzam and his followers said: He is not capable of the ugly act (al-fi'l al-qabih), because with knowledge of its ugliness it is foolishness, and below that ignorance, and both are deficiencies from which He, exalted, must be purified. The answer: There is no ugliness relative to Him, for everything is His property, so He may dispose of it in whatever way He wills. And even if the ugliness of the act relative to Him is granted, its utmost is non-occurrence due to the presence of a deterrent (al-sarif) from it, namely ugliness, and that does not negate capability over it. His saying: 'And otherwise he would be good and evil together' — it is clear that their argument, even if complete, does not prove their claim, because it only negates the doing of evil, while the claim is the negation of capability over it, and it is obvious that one capable of evil who does not do it is not evil. His saying: 'And the answer: we accept the consequent' — this is on the assumption that by 'good' is meant creator of good, and by 'evil' creator of evil; but if by 'good' is meant one whose good predominates over his evil, and by 'evil' one whose evil predominates over his good, then the answer is the denial of the implication, and since the detail was given earlier, he suffices here with what he mentioned. His saying: 'the demand of his nature and the predominant in his habit' — al-nahiza with open nun, unpointed ha, and pointed zay, on the pattern fa'ila; and al-hijir with kasr of ha, shadda on jim, and fatha of ra; it has other dialects: al-hijir on the pattern fasiiq, al-ihjiri with kasr of hamza and fatha of ra, and hijriyyan with kasr of hamza and ra and shadda on ya. His saying: 'Because with knowledge of its ugliness it is foolishness' — it is said: foolishness only follows on the assumption that the act is ugly relative to Him, when the aspect of goodness is not strongly preponderant; for then wisdom requires that act to occur so that its great goodness is attained even if slight ugliness follows, as the philosophers said: evil enters into decree by way of subordination. This only refutes them if they are not speaking about absolute ugliness or that whose aspect of ugliness is strongly preponderant. His saying: 'And that does not negate capability over it' — for if you say: Rather it negates it, because the capable (al-maqdur) for us is what can be done or left, so if the ugly were capable on the assumption of ugliness relative to Him, then the possibility of deficiency would follow — exalted is He above that greatly — I say: The problem here follows from external impossibility (al-imtina' al-ghayri), i.e., from the attribute of ugliness external to the essence of the act, not from itself, so it does not contradict the essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) sufficient for being capable (al-maqduriyya).
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents al-Nazzam's argument and the refutation. al-Nazzam claims that God cannot perform ugly acts (القبيح) because doing so knowingly would be foolish, and ignorantly would be ignorance, both deficiencies. The answer first states that ugliness is relative: nothing is ugly relative to God because all is His property. Even if ugliness is granted relative to Him, it only prevents the act from occurring, not the capability itself. Then the commentary (قوله) clarifies various points: the dualists' confusion between capability and actual doing; the correct interpretation of 'good' and 'evil'; lexical notes on words; a further response that ugliness does not entail foolishness if there is greater good; and finally a distinction between external impossibility and essential possibility, preserving capability.

Key Terms
الفرقة الرابعة
النظام
فعل قبيح
سفه
نقص
صارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an act is considered ugly, God cannot be capable of it.
Correction: The passage shows that ugliness relative to God does not negate capability; it only deters the act.

Checkpoint
What is the primary answer to al-Nazzam's claim that God cannot do ugly acts?
A) God does not know what is ugly.
B) There is no ugliness relative to God because everything is His property.
C) God only does good acts.
D) Ugliness is subjective and does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nazzam's argument fails because ugliness is relative and does not affect God's capability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that feels unjust or ugly, do you truly believe that nothing is ugly relative to God? How does this lesson challenge your immediate emotional reaction? Will you surrender your judgment to His ownership today?
?
Al-Nazzam argued that if God knew an act was ugly, doing it would be foolish. Do you ever secretly think God's actions in your life are foolish? What would it take for you to fully trust that His knowledge and ownership make every act of His wise, even when you cannot see it? Commit to a specific situation where you will replace judgment with trust.
?
The lesson teaches that ugliness only prevents the occurrence, not the capability. Are there areas in your life where you confuse God's non-action (something He does not do) with His inability? How does knowing that He has full capability but chooses not to act change your perspective? What does that choice reveal about His wisdom and mercy toward you?

Clarifying Capability and Impossibility
Al-Iji addresses further objections and clarifies key distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between capability and actual doing, the interpretation of 'good and evil', and the difference between essential and external impossibility.

Core Idea
Capability remains even if the act is not done; external impossibility does not negate essential possibility.

Building on the previous refutation, al-Iji addresses further points: he distinguishes between capability and actual performance, clarifies the meaning of 'good' and 'evil' in theological discourse, and introduces the crucial distinction between essential and external impossibility. This lesson unpacks those fine points.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish capability from actual doing
Notice that al-Nazzam's argument confuses not doing an evil act with not being able to do it. The passage shows that one can be capable without acting.
2
Interpret 'good' and 'evil' correctly
Al-Iji explains two possible meanings for 'good' and 'evil': as creator of good/evil, or as one whose good/evil predominates. The refutation depends on the interpretation.
3
Consider the wisdom argument
Even if an act is ugly relative to God, it may be done for a greater good, so foolishness does not necessarily follow.
This is a subsidiary point.
4
Grasp essential vs. external impossibility
The final distinction is crucial: ugliness is an external attribute that makes the act impossible to occur (imtina' ghayri), but the act itself has essential possibility (imkan dhati) which is sufficient for being within God's power.
This resolves the paradox of capability over acts that are never done.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?
